Received 
      30th November 2020
    First published on 12th April 2021AbstractPeriodic nanostructures, a group of nanomaterials consisting of single or multiple nano units/components periodically arranged into ordered patterns (e.g., vertical and lateral superlattices), have attracted tremendous attention in recent years due to their extraordinary physical and chemical properties that offer a huge potential for a multitude of applications in energy conversion, electronic and optoelectronic applications. Recent advances in the preparation strategies of periodic nanostructures, including self-assembly, epitaxy, and exfoliation, have paved the way to rationally modulate their ferroelectricity, superconductivity, band gap and many other physical and chemical properties. For example, the recent discovery of superconductivity observed in “magic-angle” graphene superlattices has sparked intensive studies in new ways, creating superlattices in twisted 2D materials. Recent development in the various state-of-the-art preparations of periodic nanostructures has created many new ideas and findings, warranting a timely review. In this review, we discuss the current advances of periodic nanostructures, including their preparation strategies, property modulations and various applications.
Hang Yin
Hang Yin is currently a PhD student at the Research School of Chemistry, Australian National University (Australia). He got his MSc from the University of Macau (China) in 2018 and BSc from Sichuan University (China) in 2015. His research interests lie in supramolecular chemistry, molecular machines, and energy conversion. He is now focusing on the development of novel nano materials for photocatalytic energy conversion.
Kaijian Xing
Dr Kaijian Xing is a postdoc at ARC Centre of Excellent in Future Low-Energy Electronics Technologies at Monash University. He started Master study on nano-electronic engineering at the University of Melbourne. Afterwards he obtained the PhD degree in condensed matter physics at La Trobe University. He has been working on diamond electronics for the past 4 years, developing a solid-state surface transfer doping approach for controlling the diamond surface conductivity. The recent breakthrough made by him in the heterostructure between diamond and transition metal oxides paves the way for the high-performance diamond surface conducting devices as well as the spintronics.
Yurou Zhang
Yurou Zhang received her BEng degree from East China University of Science and Technology in 2019. Since then, she has been pursuing her PhD degree under the supervision of Dr Jung Ho Yun at the University of Queensland. Her research interests mainly focus on optoelectronic properties and applications of organic–inorganic hybrid perovskite materials.
Jung-Ho Yun
Jung-Ho Yun is currently an ARC DECRA Fellow in UQ Nanomaterials Centre, School of Chemical Engineering, the University of Queensland, Australia. He received his BSc and MSc degrees from Kyung Hee University, Republic of Korea, and PhD degree from the University of New South Wales, Australia in 2012. Prior to the PhD study, he also worked as a researcher to develop functional nanomaterials-based electrodes for electrochemical wastewater treatment systems at R&D Centre, NANOPAC Co., Ltd., Republic of Korea for 5 years. His research interests lie in designing nanostructured semiconducting materials toward solar energy conversion, environmental remediation, and electronic sensing applications.
Dong-Chen Qi
Dongchen Qi is an Associate Professor and ARC Future Fellow at Queensland University of Technology. He received his BSc in Physics from Peking University (2003) and PhD in Physics from the National University of Singapore (2009). His research interests lie in the area of experimental condensed matter physics and surface science, focusing on creating, understanding, and controlling the surfaces and interfaces of functional materials at the nanoscale to develop new technologies and material platforms for the next-generation devices.
Zongyou Yin
Associate Professor Zongyou Yin obtained his BS and MS degrees at Jilin University in China, and completed his PhD at Nanyang Technological University (NTU) in Singapore. Then, he started his postdoc careers at NTU/Singapore, IMRE/Singapore, followed by MIT and then Harvard University. Dr Yin started his own research group at Australian National Univesrity (ANU) from 2017. His group's research is interdisciplinary, encompassing the chemistry and physics of nano-to-atomic materials, fundamental relationship among materials-structures-devices, and synergistic integration of multi-functions towards systems for energy and wearable applications.
1. Introduction
The progress of human civilization is in line with the development of materials. From the Stone Age to Bronze and from Bronze to Iron, the application of these materials brought a revolution to the farming tools, accelerating the efficiency of agricultural production. The application of rubber to sealing steam and metals for electricity are two key inventions during the first and second industrial revolutions, respectively. Decades ago, the development of semiconducting materials and their advances in electronic and optoelectronic device technologies significantly influenced modern life. Nowadays, it is the era of nanotechnology, and various nanomaterials have been developed and applied to different areas in catalysis,1–4 biotechnology,5–7 energy conversion,8–10 energy storage,8,9,11,12etc.,13,14 regardless of their composition and morphology.15–18 Among these nanomaterials, periodic nanostructures (PNSs), including notable superlattices, have attracted wide attention from the scientific community due to their periodic ordering that offers unique properties for various applications.
Periodic structures in general consist of several or more regularly repeating identical arrays of nano, micro, or macroscale entities with a geometric shape in an orderly patterned manner. Often, these entities organize along one or several independent directions, and generate short- or long-range ordered assemblies with different configurations and dimensionalities on all scales. In recent years, there has been a growing research interest in the rational design and fabrication of periodic nanomaterials, owing to their potential in a wide range of properties, and further applications by the virtue of periodic orderings at the nanoscale.19–21 In contrast to micro- or macro-scale periodicity, nano-scale periodicity strongly influences the electronic, optical and other properties of the material,22–26 and offers distinct collective characteristics that may be very different from those of their bulk counterparts.27–29 Especially, the discovery of the superconducting graphene bilayer moiré superlattice has brought PNSs to a new level.30 Benefitting from the advantages of these properties, the applications of PNSs in energy conversion,20 and in photoelectronic and electronic devices19,31,32 have been intensively studied.
The concept of PNSs covers a wide range of materials, such as traditional III–V group superlattices,33,34 metal oxide superlattices,24,35 periodic layered materials,28,29 and nanoparticle based periodic arrays.36,37 A number of review papers have discussed some of these PNSs according to their dimensions and compositions, such as reviews on oxide superlattices,38 chemical synthesized 2D superlattices,19 van der Waals 2D material-based superlattices,21 and 2D layered PNSs.20 However, there is still a lack of an overview of PNSs with integrating their preparation strategies, properties, and applications.
Therefore, in this review, we will summarize the advances in periodic nanostructured materials, including their preparation methods, properties, and applications. Herein, we will begin the review by discussing different preparation strategies, including vapor phase preparation, solution phase preparation and other complex preparation methods, which provide the platform for further in-depth studies. Then, the properties of the periodic nanostructured materials, ranging from ferroelectricity, ferromagnetism, piezoelectricity and superconductivity to the electronic, optoelectronic, and optical properties, will be discussed. Finally, we will summarize their applications in energy conversion and (opto)electronic devices, and then present the challenges and opportunities of PNSs for future study.
2. Preparation
With the advancements in PNSs over the past decades, the preparation of PNSs with precisely controlled morphologies at the large scale is still a key challenge that limits their wide range of applications. So far, various preparation strategies (Table 1) were attempted for PNSs with different morphologies, components and applications.

2.1. Vapor phase preparation
Vapor phase preparation is widely used to modify the substrate surface, and is also one of the most important methods used to synthesize PNSs. The vapor phase preparation can be divided into chemical vapor deposition and physical vapor deposition. The two separate deposition methods will be summarized and discussed in the following subtitles.

2.1.1. Chemical vapor deposition. 
Chemical vapor deposition (CVD) is a general method used to deposit one or more chemical gases or vapors onto the substrate surface to synthesize thin films or nanomaterials.39 In recent years, much progress has been made in fabricating lateral or vertical hetero-nanostructures from CVD epitaxial deposition based on using the lattice mismatch between the multiple crystalline films. These hetero-nanostructures have the potential to be used in electronics and optoelectronics by modulating the electronic properties of ultrathin 2D nanosheets.40
In fabricating two different kinds of transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDs) monolayers, mono- and multilayer nanosheets of TMDs initially deposit on the SiO2 surface via CVD method.41–43 Subsequently, the second layer epitaxially grows on the edge or surface of the existing TMD layer.44–50 It is important to have a fresh, unpassivated active edge to grow the second layer. Recently, different gas-phase deposition methods have been developed to fabricate lateral and vertical epitaxial hetero-nanostructures of TMD nanosheets. Duan and co-workers reported a CVD-based epitaxial growth technique in preparing lateral hetero-nanostructures composed of WSe2–WS2 and MoSe2–MoS2 by in situ switching the vapor-phase reactants without exposing the active edges to ambient conditions.44 Some other studies demonstrated that tuning the growth temperature45 and the growth time46 are effective approaches to control the growth of MoS2–WS2 heterostructures. By using the step-by-step CVD process, 2D ternary heterostructures (WS2–WSe2–MoS2 and WS2–MoSe2–WSe2), instead of 2D binary heterostructures, were synthesized (Fig. 1).51 In addition, the repeated operation of the same method yielded an A–B–A–B type PNS, i.e., WS2–WSe2–WS2–WSe2 superlattice.
 Fig. 1  (a) Schematic illustration of the preparation process of the (b) single component lateral nanosheet, (c) dual components lateral heterostructure, (d) triple components lateral heterostructure, and (e) dual components lateral PNSs via CVD. Reprinted with permission from ref. 51. Copyright 2017, AAAS Publishing. 
The additional modifications to the CVD method can also yield PNSs. For instance, Geohegan and co-workers prepared patterned arrays of the monolayer MoSe2–MoS2via lateral epitaxial heterostructures for the first time.52 As given in Fig. 2, a monolayer of MoSe2 was first deposited by CVD method, and then it was masked by conventional patterning processes, following the pulsed laser vaporization of sulphur to selectively convert the unmasked MoSe2 areas to MoS2 in triangles, circles and ribbons.
 Fig. 2  Schematic illustration of the preparation process of the MoSe2–MoS2 lateral periodic arrays. Reprinted with permission from ref. 52. Copyright 2015, Springer Nature Publishing. 


2.1.2. Physical vapor deposition. 
Physical vapor deposition (PVD) shares the similar process with CVD. However, there is no chemical reaction during the preparation process, and the chemical vapor directly deposits on the target substrate. Various heterostructures were prepared based on the inorganic,53–56 organic,57–59 and inorganic–organic hybrids60,61via PVD technique. Many organic related materials prepared by PVD showed PNSs due to the self-assembly via supramolecular interactions.62
The preparation of PNSs via PVD method involves different supramolecular interactions, including van der Waals interaction, hydrogen bonding, halogen bonding, intermolecular π–π interaction, and electrostatic interaction. These interactions influence the construction of PNSs, such as the direct formation of backbones and accommodation of additional components.62 For instance, the vapor phase sequential deposition of cobalt(II) hexadecafluoro-29H,31H-phthalocyanine (F16CoPc) and nickel(II) tetraphenyl-21H,23H-porphine (NiTPP) formed densely packed, well-ordered 2D crystalline regions with 1:1 composition on Au(111) through fluorine-phenyl interactions.63 The ordered 2D bimolecular self-assembled 2D regions extended about 100 nm, covering 25% of the surface. The CoPc and NiTPP prepared by coadoption in an ultra-high vacuum (UHV) showed a densely packed, well-defined bimolecular structure, but with a disordered composition.64 The H-bonded honeycomb-like porous network of melamine-PTCDI on a silver-terminated silicon surface in which the pores encapsulated C60 is an example for a well-defined three-component system.65 The hexagonal pores of the binary melamine-PTCDI network consisting of melamine in the vertices and PTCDI in the straight lines accommodated heptametric C60 by inducing the co-location of the several C60 molecules. This new surface phase served as a template for the formation of an additional C60 layer on the hexagonal network of the melamine-PTCDI framework. In another study, chloro[subphthalocyaninato]boron(III) (SubPc) and C60 were self-assembled to well-ordered 2D binary monolayers on Ag(111) in UHV.66 Two phases of the 2D PNSs were constructed: (1) a 2D periodic “stripe” phase consisting of alternate 1D quasilinear chains of C60 and zigzag rows of SubPc, and (2) a 2D hexagonal “star” phase of the C60 trimer surrounded by three SubPc units (Fig. 3).
 Fig. 3  (a) Molecular structures of SubPC and C60. (b) Artificially colored STM image of the basic unit of the “stripe” pattern. (c) Artificially colored STM image of PNSs based on SubPC and C60. (d) STM image showing two different phases on Ag(111). (e) STM image of the 2D hexagonal “star” phase. Reprinted with permission from ref. 66. Copyright 2002, John Wiley and Sons. 
Molecular beam epitaxy (MBE) is a kind of PVD method, which is conducted under UHV condition. MBE method can precisely control the deposition/epitaxy at the atomic or molecular level,67 which can be ascribed to the effusion cells that can generate a “molecular beam” with low interparticle collisions before reaching the substrate surface, enabling it to produce superlattices. For instance, the PbSeTe/PbTe quantum dot superlattice,68 Si0.7Ge0.3/Si superlattice,69 and Bi2Te3/Sb2Te3 superlattice70 (Fig. 4) were all synthesized by MBE method.
 Fig. 4  (a) High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) micrograph recorded on the SL structure and corresponding Fourier transform ([110] zone axis). The (003), (006), and (009) reflections are marked by crosses. Asterisks mark the superlattice reflections. (b) and (c) High-angle annular dark field-scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) micrographs of the as-grown sample. Reprinted with permission from ref. 70. Copyright 2014, American Chemical Society. 
Pulsed laser deposition (PLD) is another kind of PVD method. The high-power laser beam is applied to assist the evaporation and ionization processes of target materials that will be used to deposit on the substrate to form thin films.67 The PLD process can occur not only in UHV, but also in the presence of background gas, such as O2, which is suitable for the deposition of oxide films. Hence, PLD is widely used to synthesize metal oxide-based vertical PNSs via alternate deposition.35,71–74 For instance, LaNiO3/La0.7Sr0.3MnO3,72 BiFeO3/Ce-doped-LaMnO3 (Fig. 5),73 LaMnO3/SrIrO3,71 and BiFeO3/BiMnO335 can be prepared on the SrTiO3(001) substrate, and MgTi2O4/SrTiO3 can be deposited on the (001)-oriented cubic spinel MgAl2O4 substrate,74 respectively, via PLD method.
 Fig. 5  Schematic of the epitaxial BiFeO3 thin film on the SrTiO3 (001) substrate fabricated by alternately depositing BiFeO3 and Ce-doped LaMnO3 layers. Reprinted with permission from ref. 71. Copyright 2020, AIP Publishing. 

2.2. Solution phase preparation
The solution phase preparation method is widely applied for the large-scale preparation of PNSs. In addition, it is an important supplementary preparation method to vapor phase preparation. In this section, solution phase preparation strategies (including intercalation and exfoliation methods to prepare 2D materials), which act as precursors for 2D PNSs and intercalated PNSs will be discussed, and followed with the self-assembly methods for 1D, 2D, and 3D periodic nanomaterials.

2.2.1. Intercalation and exfoliation. 
2D materials are essential precursors for the preparation of 2D layered/based PNSs, but the low yield efficiency of vapor based preparation methods limits the synthesis of 2D layered PNSs based on 2D monolayers. Liquid exfoliation of 2D materials from their bulk counterparts is the general method used to produce 2D materials in a large scale. The critical challenge in exfoliating 2D nanosheets from their bulk materials is overcoming the van der Waals forces between the adjacent layers. The liquid exfoliation has two general routes, and the first route is direct exfoliation in various solvents with the assistance of ultrasonication. This route has been used for the production of graphene, h-BN, and TMDs, and others.75–77 The solvent can minimize the energy of exfoliation. However, it still faces the difficulties to produce 2D monolayer materials in high yield.
Intercalation of ions into the interlayers of the bulk-layered material is another general process applied prior to exfoliation in preparation of 2D materials in the large scale. After the interaction step, the van der Waals forces decrease due to the enlarged spacing between the adjacent layers. Although the intercalation of ions may introduce additional impurities and induce a complex purification process, exfoliation after the interaction of the ions is still one of the mainstream methods, and is widely used to prepare various 2D nanosheets, such as notable TMD nanosheets and LDH nanosheets.78 As shown in Fig. 6, Li+ and related reagents are the first choice to be intercalated into the interlayers of bulk materials. The intercalation of Li+ can not only expand the interlayer spaces to weaken the van der Waals interactions, the products of Li(OH)2 and H2 gas that are produced from the reaction of metallic Li and water will further push apart the layers to accelerate the exfoliation process.78
 Fig. 6  Electrochemical lithiation process for the fabrication of 2D nanosheets from the layered bulk material. Reprinted with permission from ref. 78. Copyright 2011, John Wiley and Sons. 
As discussed, the exfoliation process is an essential step prior to further assembling the 2D layered PNSs. However, as a derivative process, the ion-intercalated bulk layered materials can also be transferred to ion/nanoparticle-confined PNSs by exchanging ions with other nanoparticles.79–81 This method is convenient for modulating the spacing between the sheets to further study the effect of the interlayer distance on the activity of the layered materials,79 and also to introduce external active materials to further enhance their performances.80,81


2.2.2. Self-assembly. 
Self-assembly is a powerful and versatile bottom-up synthetic approach that generates structurally-highly organized periodic aggregates from molecular to macroscopic sized components. Various non-covalent interactions, such as hydrogen bonding, electrostatic interactions, π-stacking, van der Waals interaction, metal–metal interactions, hydrophobic interactions, and others, are usually involved in the assembly process.20,62,82,83 Coordination-driven self-assembly, such as metal coordination bonding between the electron-rich end groups of organic linkers and transition-metal atoms, is also an appropriate bonding for self-assembly.83,84 Self-assembly relies on the chemical complementarity and structural compatibility of the starting materials. Therefore, by rationally designing and fabricating the starting materials with complementary shapes and chemical/physical properties, the structure of the final assembly can be encoded in the starting materials. Thus, preconceived self-assembled structures can be fabricated under appropriate reaction conditions.20,82,84 Integrating different concepts and synthetic strategies, enormous complexity and diversity can be introduced to the self-assembled nanostructures.
2.2.2.1. One-dimensional PNSs. 
One-dimensional (1D) materials usually refer to nanowires, which have been intensively studied for various applications. Nanowire can be applied in different areas, such as electronic and optical devices.32,85 In particular, periodic nanostructured nanowires may demonstrate enhanced performances due to their multiple periodic components.86 Therefore, different preparation strategies have been developed to prepare periodic nanowires via solution-based self-assembly process.
A variety of supramolecular interactions, such as electrostatic interaction, π–π stacking and others, can contribute to the construction of 1D PNSs in solution phase via self-assembly. π–π Stacking is widely used for the construction of organic 1D nanowires based on planar π-conjugated molecules.87 π–π Stacking is more suitable for preparing homogeneous nanostructures, rather than PNSs, due to the nature of the π-interactions that have no disparity towards various molecules. π–π Stacking may also construct heterostructures, but it is hard to control their periodicity. Electrostatic interactions are another important type of interactions used to build up supramolecular 1D nanostructures.88 Mirkin et al. reported on a 1D colloidal superlattice based on triangular Au nanoprisms and cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB) via the balance between the attractive depletion forces and repulsive electrostatic forces.89 This 1D superlattice demonstrated a large spacing distance, which is four times the thickness of a single nanoprism. Zheng et al. proposed a method to produce a Pd nanosheets based 1D Pd superlattice nanowire.90 Different from previous work that used depletion force to balance the electrostatic repulsion, the authors introduced counter-cations (e.g., Fe3+, Al3+) to decrease the repulsive electrostatic force between the negatively charged Pd nanosheets.90 This method offered a controllable length of Pd superlattice nanowire (ranging from 25 nm to 283 nm). By controlling the concentration of the counter-cations in solution, the length of the Pd superlattice nanowire can be increased as a result of the increasing ratio of counter-cations (Fig. 7a–c). It is easy to construct 1D nanowires on planar molecules and 2D nanosheets since the interaction forces align with the growth direction of the nanowires. However, it is challenging to assemble nanowires on nanocrystals (NCs) due to their isotropic nature, which induces nonobvious directional interactions.91 Hence, external assistance is necessary to support the construction of 1D nanowires based on NCs. Dong et al. raised a general method for the preparation of a 1D NC superlattice based on NCs with the assistance of molecular clusters.92 The preparation method yields single-NC-wide polymer-like flexible linear chains containing up to ∼1000 close-packed NCs (with the length exceeding 18 μm), as shown in Fig. 7d–g. This method is suitable for a wide range of hydrophobic NCs, regardless of their composition, size, and shape. It is believed that the balance of solvophobicity and solvophilicity dictates the growth of the 1D NCs superlattice.
 Fig. 7  TEM images of the Pd superlattice nanowires with a Fe3+/Pd ratio of (a) 0.05, (b) 0.1, and (c) 1. Reprinted with permission from ref. 90. Copyright 2014, American Chemical Society. (d) Schematic illustration of the solution based self-assembly process of the 1D NC superlattice. (e) High-magnification TEM image of a segment of a straight NC chain and the corresponding intensity profile (inset) along the chain length (indicated by the arrow). (f) TEM image of an ultralong NC chain. (g) High-magnification TEM image of the region indicated in (f). Reprinted with permission from ref. 92. Copyright 2016, American Chemical Society. 
2.2.2.2. Two-dimensional PNSs. 
Two-dimensional (2D) periodic nanomaterials include lateral single-layered periodic nanomaterials and vertical multi-layered periodic nanomaterials. The single-layered 2D periodic nanomaterials can be prepared by direct assembly from small molecules, while the vertical PNSs are prepared by assembly from single-layered materials yielded by exfoliation process.
A wide range of molecules have been self-assembled to 2D periodic supramolecular assemblies by employing directional intermolecular hydrogen bonding (H-bonding) as the driven force for the molecular recognition process.93–96 The position and the number of functionalities that can form H-bonding strongly influence the structural and functional diversity of the ultimate H-bonded assembly. Trimesic acid (TMA) is an interesting H-bond linker molecule that forms H-bonded supramolecular motifs between the carboxylic groups either with cyclic dimer- or trimer-configuration due to its planer aromatic core with 3-fold carboxylic acids.
Self-assembly of TMA forms a 2D-periodic grid architecture with two different phases on a solid interface; a six-fold “chicken-wire structure” and three-fold “flower-like structures”, which are different in terms of the H-bonding pattern.93,94 The periodic cavities in the H-bonded 2D template of TMA form various host–guest multicomponent nanostructures via host–guest complexation. For example, Fig. 8 demonstrates that the TMA-coronene supramolecular hybrid was fabricated by introducing guest coronene (COR) molecules (diameter of ∼1 nm) into the hexagonal cavities of the chicken-wire TMA hexagonal cell monolayer at 1-octanoic acid/single-layer graphene supported by SiO2.93
 Fig. 8  TMA–COR hybrid assemblies on the single-layer graphene/SiO2. (a) Chemical structure of TMA. (b) Dimeric and trimeric form of TMA. (c) High-resolution STM image and (d) proposed mechanism of the formation of the chicken-wire structure of the host TMA by guest coronene molecules. Reprinted with permission from ref. 93. Copyright 2014, the Royal Society of Chemistry. 
The self-assembly of C60 and oligothiophene macrocycles in H-bonded TMA templated 2D network at the liquid–solid interface of heptanoic acid solution and highly oriented pyrolytic graphite (HOPG) was also studied with scanning tunnelling microscopy and spectroscopy.94 Here, the Saturn-like complexes of C60 with oligothiophene macrocycles in mono- and bilayers have also been epitaxially assembled on the H-bonded 2D-host template of TMA at the interface of heptanoic acid solution and HOPG via double hosting the C60 molecules by non-covalent interactions.94 The hexagonal cavities of the chicken-wire structure of the 2D TMA monolayer on the liquid/graphite interface with the pore diameter around 12 Å provides the host sites for the epitaxial ordering of the π-expanded macrocyclic oligothiophenes hosting C60 molecules. Here, the self-assembly of the Saturn-like complex was driven by the site recognition of C60 molecules in the templated monolayer of TMA. This study demonstrates that the H-bonding and charge transfer interactions between the donor–acceptor systems can be used to manipulate the electronic properties of the final nano-assembly.
The diverse range of 1,3,5-tris(10-carboxydecyloxy) benzene (TCDB)-based multicomponent architectures are also constructed by encapsulating various guest species into the tetragonal cavities of the 2D, hydrogen-bonded TCBD host network on HOPG by coadsorption approach with scanning tunneling microscopy (STM) under ambient conditions.95 For example, the inclusion of copper phthalocyanine (CuPc) and coronene as the guest species to the cavities of the host network of TCBD on HOPG formed well-ordered CuPc/TCDB and coronene/TCDB host–guest architectures in large areas.95 Another study demonstrated that cavities of the 2D network of TCBD on HOPG could accommodate vanadyl phthalocyanine (VOPc) as a guest molecule to construct 2D VOPc/TCBD host–guest architectures.96 Interestingly, the VOPc/TCBD showed thermal annealing induced phase transformation from the VOPC dimer entrapped VOPc/TCDB (II) phase to the monomer-entrapped VOPc/TCDB (I) phase.96 As illustrated in Fig. 9, it was also found that different unsubstituted metallophthalocyanines (MPc), such as H2Pc, CuPc, VOPc, and ClGaPc, and halogen-substituted MPcs (such as F16CuPc) could be encapsulated in the porous H-bonded network of formed MPc/TCDB architectures.97
 Fig. 9  (a) High-resolution STM (HR-STM) image (649.7 mV, 399.8 pA) of the monomer-entrapped H2Pc/TCDB (I). (b) HR-STM image of (−573 mV, 470 pA) F16CuPc/TCDB (I). (c) and (d) Proposed models of H2Pc/TCDB (I) and F16CuPc/TCDB (I), respectively. Reprinted with permission from ref. 97. Copyright 2007, American Chemical Society. 
The hydrogen bonded porous network of TCBD can also be used to entrap different helical and saddle oligothiophenes on HOPG. Four different nonplaner-ligothiophene molecules, i.e., helical (TMS)2-[7]helicene (T1), naphthalene-cored double (TMS)2-[7]helicene (T2), saddle-shaped cyclooctatetrathiophene (T3) and its derivative α,α,α,α-tetraphenyl-cyclooctatetrathiophene (T4), were encapsulated into the pre-formed porous TCBD-host template to construct TCBD_T1, TCBD_T2, TCBD_T3 and TCBD_T4 on HOPG by using STM under ambient conditions.98 The structure of the TCBD-T1 to T4 strongly depends on the balancing of the H-bonding of the TCBD network, and the other non-covalent interactions between the host-TCBD and guest molecules.
So far, we discussed two component host–guest supramolecular porous networks comprising a single type of host molecule, in which the pores can accommodate guest molecules with specific sizes. However, there are very few experimental studies on multicomponent, periodic host–guest supramolecular networks composed of two or more kinds of host molecular components at the liquid–solid interface. These multicomponent assemblies possess cavities with different sizes/shapes that could entrap appropriate guest species via size- and shape-selective guest inclusion. Therefore, optimized recognition and selection processes are important in the hierarchical self-assembly of a monolayer with preferential binding sites from multiple components, and in controlling the host–guest complexation within the monolayer to accommodate various guest species. The first four-component 2D crystalline architecture was successfully self-assembled from a Kagomé-type heterocomplex-stabilized nanoporous network of bisDBA-C12, which hosted a COR1-ISA6 cluster and TRI molecules in the hexagonal and triangular pores, respectively, at the 1-octanoic acid–HOPG interface with STM at room temperature.99 Here, the control manipulation of the size and shape of the guest molecules/clusters with the nanopores of the Kagomé patterned host complex was successfully applied as the design strategy. Another four-component 2D crystalline architecture was reported from the host molecules of TCDB, host 1,2,17,18,33,34-hexaaza-[2,6](4,40)cyclophane-1,17,33-triene (3NN-Macrocycle), and guest molecules of coronene and heptanoic acid at a liquid–solid interface on HOPG.100 TCBD and 3NN-macrocycles formed the binary hybrid host network with two different cavities, hexagonal (A) and triangular (B), with sizes of about 1.3 and 0.8 nm, respectively. Cavity A encapsulated the coronene, while B accommodated heptanoic acid, featuring the site- and size-selective effects.
A mixed 2D alternate bimolecular adlayer with two domains of copper(II) tetraphenyl-21H,23H-porphine (CuTPP) and cobalt(II) phthalocyanine (CoPc) were on Au(100)-(hex) in benzene solution (Fig. 10). In contrast to the compositionally disordered CoPc and NiTPP molecular assembly prepared under UHV conditions, these assemblies showed 2D, well-organized, alternate molecular arrays (around 50 nm), which ran nearly parallel to the atomic lattice direction of the Au(100) substrate.101
 Fig. 10  (a) HR-STM image of the CoPc and CuTPP periodic nanostructure on the Au(100)-(hex) surface, and (b) height-shaded view. (c) Structural model of the CoPc and CuTPP periodic nanostructure. Reprinted with permission from ref. 101. Copyright 2003, American Chemical Society. 
The formation of binary adlayers of CoPc and CuTPP were also investigated on Au(100)-(hex) and Au(111) by cyclic voltammetry (CV) and in situ STM. The binary supramolecular adlayer of CoPc/CuTPP on Au(100)-(hex) showed well-ordered 2D, striped regions consisting of alternately arranged binary molecular rows. In contrast, the mixed adlayer on Au(111) showed well-ordered regions with 1:1 composition of CoPc and CuTPP, but under different concentrations. This study demonstrated that the supramolecular assembly of CoPc and CuTPP on Au depends on its crystallographic orientation, as well as the concentrations of the constituent molecules.102
As shown in Fig. 11, Xue fabricated 1D patterned four component self-assembled monolayers (SAM) comprising an alkadiyne side chain bearing anthracene derivatives via the alkadiyne side chain shape-dependent molecular recognition strategy (anthracene derivatives differ from the side chain length and the side chain position of the first alkyne carbon).103 Here, the close packing of the specific pairs of shape and length complementary alkadiyne side chains forms the monolayer through van der Waals interactions at the phenyloctane solution-HOPG interface. The monolayer exhibits a periodic patterning with alternating columns of aryl cores and aliphatic chains, and the unit cell contains six anthracene derivatives in the order S1–D1–D2–S2–D2–D1.
 Fig. 11  (a) Chemical structures of one half of the designed unit cell. (b) Image of PNSs of S1-D1-S2-D2. Reprinted with permission from ref. 103. Copyright 2012, American Chemical Society. 
The layer-by-layer method can prepare PNSs with a controllable layer number on a substrate by repeating the process of alternately sinking the substrate in two solutions that contain different materials/ions with opposite charges.20 The strong electrostatic attraction can strongly assemble the heterocomponents into PNSs. For instance, Shao et al. applied the layer-by-layer technique to prepare CoNi/Fe-PP PNSs on a quartz glass substrate.104 The increasing thickness of the CoNi/Fe-PP PNSs as a result of the growth of the layers can be directly characterized by SEM. Strongin et al. prepared MnO2 nanosheets and K+-based PNSs on a positively charged polycation (PEI)-modified fluorinated tin oxide (FTO) slide (Fig. 12).79 AFM showed a height of 2.2 ± 0.2 nm for the MnO2/K+/MnO2 bilayer, and a large interlayer spacing of 0.9 ± 0.2 nm between the two MnO2 nanosheets.
 Fig. 12  Schematic illustration of the periodic nanostructure prepared by solution-based layer-by-layer technique. Reprinted with permission from ref. 79. Copyright 2017, American Chemical Society. 
Besides the preparation methods of the 2D layered materials that require solid substrates, various solid substrate-free methods were also developed to fulfill different demands. The surface tension produced by the interface of liquid-air/liquid–liquid can provide supports for the formation of 2D layered materials. In addition, the as-produced 2D materials can be easily transported to arbitrary substrates for further applications. Murray et al. attempted to produce binary nanocrystal superlattice (BNSL) membranes at the liquid-air interface via self-assembly (Fig. 13a).36 A drop of hexane solution containing FePt and Fe3O4 nanocrystals was spread over the surface of diethylene glycol (DEG). After the evaporation of hexane, a 2D BNSL membrane was yielded on the surface of DEG. This method provides a general process that can be extended to different 2D BNSL systems, since their stoichiometry and structure can be tuned by the modulation of concentration ratios and/or size of nanocrystal components.105,106 Recently, Park et al. studied a laminar assembly polymerization (LAP) process, an interfacial synthesis technique, to produce a monolayer 2D polymer at the sharp interface between pentane and water.84 The LAP process includes three steps: (1) injection; (2) self-assembly; (3) polymerization (Fig. 13b and c). After the monomers were injected into the interlayer between pentane and water, the monomers experienced self-assembly and polymerization in the presence of bridging components to yield a 2D polymer thin film, which can be further assembled with MoS2 nanosheets to construct vertical layered PNSs.
 Fig. 13  (a) Schematic illustration of the large-scale BNSL membranes self-assembled at the liquid–air interface. Reprinted with permission from ref. 36. Copyright 2010, Springer Nature Publishing. (b) Schematic of a LAP reactor and in situ optical characterization apparatus. (c) Schematic of the LAP synthesis that involves three phases. Reprinted with permission from ref. 84. Copyright 2019, AAAS Publishing. 
The full solution-based and substrate-free method, which is based on the electrostatic interaction between the different 2D nanosheets, has been widely used for the construction of 2D layered PNSs.20 The direct mixing of positively charged nanosheets (e.g., LDHs) and negatively charged nanosheets (e.g., graphene, MoS2) can yield layer-by-layer 2D PNSs via electrostatic interaction without other assistance (Fig. 14a). For instance, NiAl LDH and reduced graphene oxide (rGO) can assemble into a NiAl/rGO periodic nanostructure in formamide;108 NiFe LDH and MoS2 can be used to construct a NiFe/MoS2 periodic nanostructure in the solution phase;107 and a CoNiFe/RuO2.1 periodic nanostructure can be yielded from the spontaneous assembly of positively charged trimetallic CoNiFe LDHs and negatively charged RuO2.1 nanosheets.109 However, there is still a demand to assemble two layered materials with the same charge into PNSs. For instance, Sasaki et al. explored a method to alter the surface charge of materials by modifying polymers with opposite charge.28 After the modification of positively charged poly(diallyldimethylammonium chloride) (PDDA), the charge of graphene changed from negative to positive, which could be subsequently used to assemble a periodic nanostructure with negatively charged MoS2 nanosheets. The PDDA of the obtained MoS2/graphene-PDDA can be removed by further annealing process (Fig. 14b).
 Fig. 14  (a) Schematic illustration of the general preparation process of the substrate free 2D layered periodic nanostructure in solution. Reprinted with permission from ref. 107. Copyright 2019, American Chemical Society. (b) Preparation method for the 2D layered periodic nanostructure based on homo-charged nanosheets. Reprinted with permission from ref. 28. Copyright 2018, American Chemical Society. 
2.2.2.3. Three-dimensional PNSs. 
As the most common morphology of nanomaterials, three-dimensional (3D) nanomaterials have been widely studied for various applications. Therefore, different preparation methods were developed to satisfy the wide range of applications. The 3D periodic nanomaterials are usually constructed from ordered, close-packed nanoparticles featuring a high surface area, abundant active sites, and collective properties that differ from the disordered/isolated nanoparticles. Since self-assembly is a general strategy to prepare the 3D periodic nanomaterials, herein the preparation methods of 3D periodic nanomaterials via self-assembly are summarized for further studies.
The spontaneous self-assembly of colloidal particles in suspensions or at the air–liquid interface provide a simple and versatile approach to form highly ordered nanoparticle arrays or structural assemblies known as colloidal superlattices with precise periodicity across large dimensions from several nanometres to micrometres.82 In comparison to the randomly configured nanoparticle mixtures, colloidal superlattices exhibit precise uniformity in particle sizes, as well as the interparticle distances, generating well-defined surface topographical patterns. The assembly of colloidal particles with nano-dimensions, such as quantum dots, nanoparticles, and nanocrystals, are interesting in the design of size-tunable colloidal superlattices.110–112 The assembly of the colloidal superlattice is, in general, driven by the colloidal forces between the individual nanoparticle that lead towards equilibrium. Therefore, various superlattice architectures can be designed by modulating the interparticle interactions and thereby tuning the internal ordering, interparticle distance, precise stoichiometry, and other parameters of the nanoparticles in the superlattice (Fig. 15). A diverse array of mono- or multiple-component colloidal superlattices consisting of either single-sized or different-sized nanoparticle combinations with various types of crystal lattices (such as binary-AB, AB2, AB5 or AB13 colloidal crystals, ternary or more complex superlattices), well-defined morphologies, including spherical and non-spherical components (such as rod, dumbbell, plate, polyhedral disc and others), and different chemical compositions, have been reported so far.36,110–113
 Fig. 15  TEM images of the binary superlattices based on different nanoparticles: (a) 13.4nm γ-Fe2O3 and 5.0nm Au; (b), 7.6nm PbSe and 5.0nm Au; (c) 6.2nm PbSe and 3.0nm Pd; (d) 6.7nm PbS and 3.0nm Pd; (e) 6.2nm PbSe and 3.0nm Pd; (f) 5.8nm PbSe and 3.0nm Pd; (g) 7.2nm PbSe and 4.2nm Ag; (h) 6.2nm PbSe and 3.0nm Pd; (i) 7.2nm PbSe and 5.0nm Au; (j) 5.8nm PbSe and 3.0nm Pd; (k) 7.2nm PbSe and 4.2nm Ag; and (l) 6.2nm PbSe and 3.0nm Pd nanoparticles. Reprinted with permission from ref. 110. Copyright 2006, Springer Nature Publishing. 
In general, 3D periodic colloidal superlattices can be mainly constructed from two different self-assembly-based synthetic strategies: (1) electrostatic self-assembly, a popular method for building PNSs in various dimensions,88,114 and (2) solvent evaporation induced self-assembly of colloidal suspensions.115 For the nanoparticles that have no particular assembly driven force to form close-packed PNSs, the evaporation of the solvent increases the concentration of nanoparticles to smoothly reduce the available free volume of each nanoparticle until the formation of close-packed PNSs (Fig. 16).37,115,116
 Fig. 16  Schematic illustration of the synthesis procedures of the CoMnO@CN superlattices via solvent evaporation self-assembly method. Reprinted with permission from ref. 37. Copyright 2015, American Chemical Society. 
DNA-linked nanoparticle superlattices have been widely studied since their size and morphology can be easily adjusted. This is due to the nature of DNA and the complementary base-pairing rule.117,118 This rule can be applied as an alternative self-assembly route to construct PNSs, as the exposed single DNA chain motif can specifically recognize and bind with its complementary DNA chain motif, thereby offering motivations to assemble hetero DNA-modified nanoparticles into PNSs.119–123 For instance, a body-centred cubic (bcc) crystalline can be constructed by DNA-functionalized nanoparticle via stepwise growth process (Fig. 17).120 The orientations of the bcc superlattices can be modulated by different contacting modes between the superlattice and substrate. In addition, this method can be used to build PNSs based on DNA-tailored nanoparticles in well order, regardless of the composition of the nanoparticles.120,123 After introducing the DNA motifs to the proteins, the DNA-modified proteins can bind with the DNA-modified Au nanoparticles to form organic–inorganic hetero superlattices.123
 Fig. 17  (a) Schematic illustration of the DNA-mediated interaction between nanoparticles. Illustrations of the superlattices grown on substrates, phase planes, 2D GISAXS scattering patterns, and SEM of the (b–e) (100)-oriented bcc superlattices and (f–i) (110)-oriented bcc superlattices, respectively. Reprinted with permission from ref. 120. Copyright 2013, John Wiley and Sons. 

2.3. Other preparation methods
Previous preparation methods have already provided popular strategies for the construction of PNSs. However, there are still some strategies that rely on multiple techniques that have not been demonstrated yet. As a supplement, this section will offer some useful methods for the preparation of PNSs.

2.3.1. Lithographic templating. 
The combination of bottom-up assembly with top-down microfabrication techniques has been applied in fabricating periodically structured materials at the nanoscale. This integrated approach combines the advantageous features of the “top-down-lithography” and “bottom-up-self-assembly” to create complex periodic architectures with well-defined size, shape, and functionality from either single or multiple components. Some studies have shown that the spontaneous self-assembly of the block copolymer (BCP) into ordered periodic structures can be integrated with advanced lithographic techniques to fabricate highly ordered densely packed nanometre-scale structures, such as nanodots, nanowires, quantum dots, nanopores, and others, on solid substrates.124–127 Here, the self-assembled nanometre-sized domains of ordered BCP thin films serve as a lithographic template to fabricate periodic nanopatterns irrespective of the chemical nature of the underlying solid substrate.128–130 The subsequent selective removal of the organic components creates the patterned structures of the metal particles, which are compatible with the periodic BCP domain ordering. Even though this approach can be successfully applied in fabricating one-component nanopatterns, creating multicomponent nanopatterns is challenging, as the geometry of the self-assembled nanodomains of BCP is generally limited to single shapes, such as spheres, cylinders, or lamellae.
Kim et al. demonstrated a multilevel block copolymer (BCP)-based self-assembly approach in fabricating laterally ordered and multicomponent periodic nanopatterns, consisting of metal nanowires and nanodots of a diverse range of metallic elements (Au, Pt, Fe, Pd, and Co) at the sub-nanometre scale (Fig. 18).131 Here, thin films of polystyrene-block-poly(4-vinylpyridine)(PS-b-P4VP) BCP were initially deposited by directed self-assembly by using a topographically prepatterned substrate of Si(100) substrate via graphoepitaxy effect. The as-deposited well-ordered surface parallel nanocylinder arrays were used as a template for the subsequent metallization. The plasma etching selectively removes the organic components, forming arrays of nanodots or nanowires of parallel cylinders. In the second cycle, PS-b-P4VP thin films were re-deposited on the metal nanowire or nanodot array, followed by the second cycle of metallization to create graphoepitaxially controlled multicomponent nanopatterns of Pd nanodot arrays, alternated with Pt nanowires or a Pt-Au binary nanodot array. This multilevel approach allows for the precise control of the lateral ordering of the metal particles, and enables the fabrication of extended-long range structures with a diverse range of pattern shapes, dimensions, and chemical compositions. Furthermore, the size of the nanocomponents was independently modified without affecting the pattern periodicity. However, a significant amount of research still needs to be conducted in order to unravel the potential of the directional self-assembly of BCP-based multicomponent nanopatterning to create different shapes and sizes.
 Fig. 18  (a) Schematic procedure of multicomponent nanopatterning. (b) The first level BCP self-assembly for surface parallel cylinders. (c) Pt metallization of parallel cylinders. (d) Second level BCP self-assembly for vertical cylinders. (e) Pd metallization of vertical cylinders to complete the Pt nanowire–Pd nanodot multicomponent nanopattern. EDS elemental mapping of the (f) Pt nanowire–Pd nanodot array and (g) Pd nanowire–Pt nanodot array. Multicomponent nanopatterns of the (h) Pt–Pd nanowire array and (i) Au–Pt nanodot array. Reprinted with permission from ref. 131. Copyright 2013, American Chemical Society. 


2.3.2. Preparation of twisted PNSs. 
Compared with the common PNSs in which periodicity comes from the array of materials, the periodicity of twisted PNSs is yielded from the twisted angle between different layers of materials. Among these, the moiré superlattice is one of the most well-known twisted PNSs. The emerging novel properties of moiré superlattices have attracted intensive studies for the preparation of twisted PNSs. The preparation of the graphene-hBN moiré superlattice is relatively easier. After the construction of the graphene-hBN bilayer heterostructure, the lattice mismatch of δ = 1.8%, and the rotational misalignment between graphene and the hBN layers, the graphene-hBN bilayer heterostructure can transform to moiré superlattices naturally.132 In contrast, the preparation of graphene-graphene bilayer moiré superlattices needs to tear the graphene flake by using the van der Waals force between hBN and graphene, and manually rotate the separated graphene pieces by a twisted angle before restacking to form graphene bilayer moiré superlattices.30 Yuan raised a water bath-assisted process to fabricate MoS2 bilayer moiré superlattices (Fig. 19).133 A MoS2 bilayer was first deposited on the SiO2 substrate, then a MoS2 bilayer accompanied with the SiO2 substrate was dropped in 2 M KOH solution at 80 °C. The hot KOH solution etched the SiO2 layer to release MoS2 film. The tensile strain promoted the released part of the MoS2 film to curve out of the plane and roll up to a nanoscroll, yielding the SiO2 bilayer moiré superlattices.
 Fig. 19  (a) Schematic illustration of the formation process of the bilayer MoS2 nanoscroll. (b) HRTEM image of the MoS2 nanoscroll. The inset is a low-magnification TEM image of the MoS2 nanoscroll. (c) Schematic diagram of the MoS2 moiré superlattice. Reprinted with permission from ref. 133. Copyright 2019, American Chemical Society. 
Besides the traditional moiré superlattices, novel preparation methods have yielded new types of twisted PNSs. Recently, Jin et al. presented a method to grow Euclidean materials on non-Euclidean surfaces,134 which is different from the traditional layered materials that grown on flat substrates. After dropping a nanoparticle on the planar surface of the substrate, a deformation process formed a cone surface instead of the planar surface. Then, the water vapor-assisted chemical vapor transport (CVT) method was applied to conduct the growth of WX2 (X = S, Se). Therefore, three conditions are present when the growth of WX2 (X = S, Se) occurs on: (1) a Euclidean surface for aligned spiral; (2) non-Euclidean cone surface for fastened spiral; (3) non-Euclidean hyperbolic cone surface for unfastened spiral (Fig. 20). Compared to the previous preparation methods for bilayer twisted materials, this method has opened up a new route to produce twisted multilayer superstructures.
 Fig. 20  (a) Triangular spiral on a Euclidean surface. (b) Triangular spiral on an unfolded non-Euclidean cone surface. (c) A cone can be folded from a circle by cutting along the radius and folding with an angle. (d) Euclidean surface for the aligned spiral; (e) non-Euclidean cone surface for the fastened spiral; (f) non-Euclidean hyperbolic cone surface for the unfastened spiral. (g) Schematic illustration of the growth process of non-Euclidean surfaces. (h) AFM image of the aligned WS2 spirals grown on the flat SiO2/Si substrate. (i–k) AFM images of the representative WS2 supertwisted spirals with various twist angles grown around the WOx particles on the SiO2/Si substrates. Reprinted with permission from ref. 134. Copyright 2020, AAAS Publishing. 

3. Properties
The ability to construct PNS in a rational fashion has provided us with a playground to induce and tailor novel physical properties and functionalities that often are absent in their parent materials. In this section, we discussed, with a focus on recent studies, the emergent physical properties of different types of PNS, most notably in the form of superlattices, including ferroelectricity, ferromagnetism, piezoelectricity and superconductivity, as well as engineered electronic/optoelectronic properties (Table 2), which are fundamentally important for exploiting PNS for future applications based on these novel artificial materials.
