3.1. Ferroelectricity, ferromagnetism and piezoelectricity

3.1.1. Ferroelectricity, ferromagnetism and multiferroics in superlattices. 
Materials that exhibit primary ferroic orders, which can be manipulated by external fields, are central to many technological applications, ranging from data storage and sensors to catalysis. Ferroelectricity is a characteristic of certain materials that have a spontaneous electric polarization, which can be reversed by the application of an external electric field, while ferromagnetism is the spontaneous orientation of magnetic moments that gives rise to ferromagnets. Ferroelectric and ferromagnetic PNS are currently the subjects of intensive research due to the interest they raise for not only technological applications, but also the fundamental scientific importance. Moreover, the coexistence of ferroelectricity and ferromagnetism in a single phase of materials yields the novel designed multiferroics,38 allowing for the manipulation of magnetic properties by an electric field in a more energy-efficient way.
Constructing artificial materials in the form of oxide superlattices has been regarded as a promising way to enhance or induce ferroelectricity, which can be precisely controlled by altering the supercells and periodicity. For example, the bismuth ferrite, BiFeO3, shows great potential in several fields, such as spintronics and ferroelectric memories, due to its versatile physics properties. However, the large leakage current greatly hampers its properties by causing spontaneous depolarization.137,138 Song et al. overcame the leakage current limits of BiFeO3 by artificially fabricating a BiFeO3/Ce-doped LaMnO3 vertical superlattices through PLD, enhancing the ferroelectricity compared with BiFeO3.73 The orthorhombic Ce-doped LaMnO3 has excellent lattice matching with the rohombohedral BiFeO3. More importantly, Ce substitution in LaMnO3 provides an efficient way to precisely control Ce doping for BiFeO3 at the atomic-scale level. This plays the key role for reducing the leakage current and enhancing the ferroelectricity of the BiFeO3/Ce-doped LaMnO3 PNS, because Ce prevents the volatility of Bi cations, as well as suppresses the reduction of Fe3+ to Fe2+.137,139 The strong interface coupling in complex oxide superlattices also helps to stabilize ferroelectricity at room-temperature in ultrathin oxide layers. For example, tunable ferroelectricity in (Sr1−αTiO3)n/BaTiO3 superlattices was reported recently by Wang et al., in which enhanced ferroelectricity was found when α ≈ 5% and n = 6 compared with their parent bulk materials.140 In addition to enhancing the ferroelectricity, Wang and his co-workers predicted the emergent ferroelectric property in SrTiO3/SrRuO3 superlattices by finely modulating the oxygen vacancy at the interface based on density functional theory (DFT) calculations, as well as kinetic Monte Carlo (KMC) simulations.141 After achieving the theoretical predication, intriguing ferroelectricity has been observed in the SrTiO3/SrRuO3 superlattices, which opens a way to induce the external physical properties, such as ferroelectricity, by defect-engineered symmetry breaking in the PNS systems.141 In addition to the magnitude of the electric polarization being modulated in the oxide superlattices, the spatial arrangement of the electric dipoles can be engineered into various types of emergent polar topologies towards the realization of magnetic skyrmion-like topological structures in ferroelectric materials (i.e., polar skyrmions), including the demonstration of chiral vortices,142,143 chiral,144 and ferroelectric bubble domains.145 More recently, Das et al. observed room-temperature nanometre-scale polar-skyrmion bubbles in [(PbTiO3)n/(SrTiO3)n]m superlattice systems, which can contribute to the advancement of ferroelectrics towards practical functionalities, such as tunable negative capacitance devices.146
Controlling the magnetic ordering in ferromagnetic materials is central to the functionalities and performance of spintronic devices. The construction of artificial oxide superlattices has also become an important method to tailor the magnetic orders via spin-correlated interfacial coupling. In 2020, Huang et al. successfully modulated the magnetic order in [(LaMn3+O3)m/(SrIrO3)m]n superlattices.71 Four superlattices (m = 8, 4, 2, 1, while n = 1, 2, 4, 8) have been fabricated by PLD, as shown in Fig. 21a. The reduction of magnetization was observed when the repeating period is shorter (Fig. 21b). Simultaneously, the magnetic hysteresis loops were noticed in all four [(LaMn3+O3)m/(SrIrO3)m]n superlattices, while the loop vanished in the reference sample (Fig. 21b). Therefore, they proved that both magnetization and coercivity can be tuned by modulating the period of the [(LaMn3+O3)m/(SrIrO3)m]n superlattices (Fig. 21c and d). They argue that the insight of this phenomena is due to the 3d/5d interface electronic correlation and the Rashba-style spin–orbit interaction. Consequently, a new route has been demonstrated for engineering the magnetic properties, which can make contributions to future developments in high-capacity storage and advanced computing applications.
 Fig. 21  (a) Schematic illustration of the [(LaMn3+O3)m/(SrIrO3)m]n superlattices. (b) Magnetization as a function of temperature measured at 0.1 T external parallel (100-direction) magnetic field. Local magnetization along out-of-plane (c) and 45° (d) measured with X-ray magnetic circular dichroism (XMCD). Reprinted with permission from ref. 71. Copyright 2020, AIP Publishing. 
Multiferroics materials with the co-existence of ferroelectric and ferromagnetic ordering have greatly attracted attention because of the coupling between ferromagnetism and ferroelectricity, which enables the control of one ferroic order with the conjugated field of the other with particular excitement of the direct electric-field control of ferromagnetism for unprecedented applications in multifunctional devices, such as energy-efficient logic and memory devices. However, it is challenging to simultaneously integrate both ferroelectricity and ferromagnetism, and induce their coupling in a singe phase of materials. Oxide superlattices provide an effective pathway to achieve the interfacial coupling between ferroelectricity and ferromagnetism through the epitaxial integration of oxide layers with distinct properties. Such designed multiferroics has recently been demonstrated by Mundy et al. in the vertical superlattices of (LuFeO3)m/(LuFe2O4)1 (Fig. 22a). This is achieved by inserting a single layer of geometrically frustrated ferrimagnetic LuFe2O4 into the matrix of geometric ferroelectric LuFeO3. The severe lattice rumpling native to LuFeO3 brings a lattice distortion to the neighboring LuFe2O4. Such an interface-induced geometric distortion enhances the magnetism of LuFe2O4 by reducing its spin frustration, while at the same time, ferroelectricity is induced in it by breaking the mirror symmetry of the LuFe2O4 layer. The magnetoelectric coupling between the ferroelectric and ferrimagnetic order in LuFe2O4 was demonstrated by the direct electric-field control of ferrimagnetism at 200 K (Fig. 22b).147 A similar strategy has been utilized recently by Jin et al. in the vertical multiferroic superlattices of (BiFeO3)m/(BiMnO3)m, as shown in Fig. 22c. The strong polarization field of the ferroelectric BiFeO3 layers exerts an induction effect on adjacent BiMnO3 layers, and causes a displacement of the Mn ions that gives rise to ferroelectric polarization in the otherwise centrosymmetric crystal structure. In contrast to the enhanced magnetism in the (LuFeO3)m/(LuFe2O4)1 case, the originally strong ferromagnetism in BiMnO3 is reduced through interfacial antiferromagnetic superexchange coupling (via Fe–O–Mn). Nevertheless, the remaining ferromagnetism co-exists with the induced ferroelectricity in a single phase of BiMnO3, which can be modulated by precisely controlling the supercell size (Fig. 22d), contributing to the practical applications of artificial multiferroic systems.35
 Fig. 22  The upper images of (a) represent the HAADF-STEM images and schematic view of the crystal structures of LuFe2O4 and LuFeO3. The lower images of (a) represent the (LuFeO3)m/(LuFe2O4)1 superlattices with m = 1–10. The left of (b) is the out-of-plane piezoresponse force microscopy results of the domain structure, following a DC bias applied to the proximal tip. The right of (b) is the X-ray magnetic circular dichroic photoemission electron microscopy image from the Fe L3 edge at 200 K. Reprinted with permission from ref. 147. Copyright 2016, Springer Nature Publishing. (c) The time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIMS) measurements of the (BiFeO3)m/(BiMnO3)m superlattices. (d) Magnetic properties of the (BiFeO3)m/(BiMnO3)m superlattices for m = 1, 2, 5, 8, 10. The upper figures of (d) show the in-plane field-dependent magnetization hysteresis curves at 10 K. The lower figures of (d) show the in-plane temperature-dependent magnetization curves at 1000 Oe. Reprinted with permission from ref. 35. Copyright 2020, the Royal Society Chemistry. 


3.1.2. Piezoelectricity in superlattices. 
Piezoelectricity represents the electric charge accumulation in certain solid materials in response to the external applied mechanical stress, or the generation of mechanical stress due to the applied voltage. Similar to ferroelectricity, the occurrence of piezoelectric properties in materials requires non-centrosymmetric crystal structures, and they often co-exist with ferroelectricity. For example, the PbTiO3/SrTiO3 heterostructure has been regarded as an ideal platform for the investigation of the electrostatic effect, and the manipulation of the superlattice periodicity and the volume fraction of PbTiO3 becomes an efficient way to modulate the physical properties of PbTiO3/SrTiO3-based superlattices.148,149 Kim et al. fabricated [(PbTiO3)6/(SrTiO3)6]5 superlattices on the SrTiO3 substrate, as shown in Fig. 23a. Fig. 23b clearly shows that the piezoelectric coefficient is greater than that of the single PbTiO3/SrTiO3 heterostructure, suggesting the [(PbTiO3)6/(SrTiO3)6]5 superlattices have more efficient piezoelectric behavior. They argue that the increase of the piezoelectric response is attributed to the local disordered polarization introduced by the interfacial coupling of the superlattice.150
 Fig. 23  (a) Schematic of the [(PbTiO3)6/(SrTiO3)6]5 superlattices on the SrTiO3 substrate with the HAAFD-STEM image of the PNS. (b) Piezoelectric coefficient d33 per unit cell of the PbTiO3 layer as a function of the DC voltage for both PbTiO3 layer and [(PbTiO3)6/(SrTiO3)6]5 PNS. Reprinted with permission from ref. 150. Copyright 2017, AIP Publishing. 

3.2. Superconductivity
Superconductivity is one of the most fascinating phenomena in condensed matter physics both due to its huge technological implications, as well as its fundamental importance. Artificial PNS offer an effective route to induce or modulate the superconductivity via interface engineering with atomic precision. The most recent breakthrough in the realization of unconventional superconductivity in twisted graphene superlattices30 at the so-called magic angle allows us to tap into superconductivity via band topology engineering, and gives rise to an emerging new field of twistronics. In this section, recent advances in the realization of superconductivity in PNS are discussed.

3.2.1. Superconductivity in complex oxide superlattices. 
Similar to the controlling of ferroic orders in complex oxide superlattices, superconductivity can also be induced or enhanced at strongly coupled complex oxide interfaces prevalent in superlattices. In 2017, the LaNiO3-based superlattice was reported as a potential platform to achieve the high-temperature superconducting state.72 Using PLD technique, a high-quality vertical superlattice with the unit of [LaNiO3 (2 u.c.)/La0.7Sr0.3MnO3 (3 u.c.)] was formed, as shown in Fig. 24a. The temperature-dependent resistance performance shows a semiconductor-type property from room temperature down to a few K, and an abrupt superconducting transition was observed at around 3.7 K (the inset of Fig. 24b) with a current of 5 μA. By applying a larger current at 0.1 mA, the superconductivity was destroyed, indicating that the superconductivity arises from the oxide superlattice. The magnetic susceptibility characterization yields the magnetic onset of superconductivity at around 3.5 K (Fig. 24c), which is consistent with the zero-resistance temperature. Furthermore, the magnetic hysteresis loop at 2 K suggests a type-II superconducting nature of this complex oxide superlattice, which is associated with a cuprate-like 3d electron occupancy through the artificial engineering of nicklate heterostructures. More recently, Hu et al. reported the intriguing observation of emergent superconductivity in the spinel insulator, MgTi2O4, by engineering a superlattice with SrTiO3. [MgTi2O4/SrTiO3] superlattices were fabricated on the MgAl2O4 substrate, and the schematic view of this superlattice is illustrated in Fig. 24d. As shown in Fig. 24e, the [MgTi2O4/SrTiO3] superlattice turns to a superconducting state between 3 K to 3.5 K, and eventually reaches the zero resistance at 3 K at zero-magnetic-field condition. The occurrence of superconductivity in MgTi2O4 is associated with the elongation of the out-of-plane lattice constant induced by the epitaxial strain. By modulating this lattice spacing (through controlling the growth temperature of the superlattice), a remarkable tuning of the superconducting transition temperature can be achieved. Intriguingly, the observed superconductivity, albeit induced by interfacial coupling, does not seem to be anisotropically confined to interfaces as typically expected at complex oxide interfaces.74 An isotropic upper critical magnetic field was observed by magnetotransport measurements with both in-plane and out-of-plane magnetic fields (Fig. 24e). They suggest that the emergence of superconductivity can be related to the suppression of orbital ordering by electron-doping effect of the non-stoichiometric Ti/Mg ratio. This work shows the great potential in further exploring the superconductivity in spinel families. However, key questions remain to be answered, such as the isotropic superconductivity behavior, which is in stark contrast to the interface conductivity observed in other oxide heterostructures.
 Fig. 24  (a) HAADF-STEM image for the [LaNiO3 (2 u.c.)/La0.7Sr0.3MnO3 (3 u.c.)] superlattices. The inset depicts the schematic of the partial atomic arrangement. (b) Resistivity plots as a function of temperature with a current of 5 μA; the inset shows the zoom-in view at the superconducting transition region. (c) Magnetic susceptibility characterization with a magnetic field of 10 Oe. The inset shows the magnetic hysteresis effect at 2 K. Reprinted with permission from ref. 72. Copyright 2018, American Chemical Society. (d) Schematic illustration of the [MgTi2O4/SrTiO3] superlattices. (e) Temperature dependence of the resistance of the [MgTi2O4/SrTiO3] superlattices under different perpendicular (upper one of (e)) and parallel (lower one of (e)) magnetic fields. Reprinted with permission from ref. 74. Copyright 2020, APS Physics Publishing. 


3.2.2. Superconductivity in intercalated van der Waals PNS. 
A hybrid vertical PNS by intercalating organic molecules into 2D van der Waals materials (inorganic materials) provides a new avenue to induce or enhance the superconductivity. Due to the intercalation of the organic species, the interactions between the inorganic layers can be significantly altered. The introduced organic intercalation layer can screen the Coulomb interaction and reduce the interlayer coupling of cooper pairs, leading to the superconductivity, or act as molecular dopants to dope electrons to the inorganic layer to enhance the superconductivity (e.g., increased Tc). For example, Shi et al. reported that by intercalating the FeSe single crystal with organic cations of tetrabutyl ammonium (TBA+) via electrochemical intercalation method, a novel hybrid vertical PNS can be formed.151 As shown in Fig. 25a, the distance between adjacent FeSe layers is increased from 0.55 nm to 1.49 nm, indicating the successful intercalation with TBA+ ions. Both transport measurements and magnetic susceptibility measurements revealed a consistent superconducting transition temperature of 50 K (Fig. 25b), which is the highest for bulk FeSe superconductors (compared with 8.9 K for pristine crystals). Furthermore, the magnetic susceptibility characterization demonstrated no flux pinning effect, indicating a very small amount of impurities or defects introduced during the intercalation process. This hybrid PNS approach provides a new pathway to induce the high-Tc superconductivity in iron-based bulk superconductors.152 A similar strategy of organic cation intercalation can be widely used in other 2D materials to form hybrid vertical PNS in which the superconducting properties, along with other exotic correlated properties, can be fashioned with atomic-scale accuracy. For example, coupling superconductivity with topological properties could open up exciting opportunities for topological quantum computation.153,154 MoTe2 and WTe2 are Weyl semimetals with many fascinating quantum properties in their monolayer limit, such as edge conduction155 and quantum spin Hall effect.156 Although the superconducting state has been reported in both gated monolayer WTe2 (Tc = 0.82 K)157 and MoTe2 (Tc = 8 K),158 it is very challenging to obtain monolayers of WTe2 or MoTe2 through mechanical exfoliation, greatly hampering the practical applications in the future. Zhang et al. recently reported a strategy to form WTe2 and MoTe2-based vertical inorganic–organic hybrid superlattices through organic cation intercalation.154Fig. 25c shows the schematic illustration of the new class of hybrid TMD intercalated with organic cations. The interlayer coupling strength was reduced after organic cation intercalation, resulting in the transition from a 3D type-II Weyl semimetal to a 3D weak topological insulator.159 The hybrid PNS sample shows larger resistance than the pristine sample at high temperature, as shown in Fig. 25d, but without the sudden change in resistance between 240 K to 260 K of the pristine sample (inset of Fig. 25d) characteristic of phase transitions, suggesting the weaker interlayer coupling effect in intercalated samples. Fig. 25e shows that the onset superconducting transition temperature of intercalated MoTe2 is 7 K, which is significantly higher than that of the corresponding pristine crystals (Tc of 0.25 K). The greatly weakened interlayer coupling by organic intercalation is also expected to lead to 2D superconductivity, which is indeed confirmed by linear R–T behavior with a characteristic 2D Berezinskii–Kosterlitz–Thouless (BKT) transition temperature of 4.7 K. Similarly, intercalated WTe2 displays an enhanced Tc of 2.3 K compared to that of 0.82 K for the monolayer gated WTe2. Consequently, hybrid vertical PNS provides an important pathway for manipulating the superconducting properties of topological 2D materials.
 Fig. 25  (a) The TEM images of FeSe and (TBA)0.3FeSe with the schematic atomic structure shown on the right. (b) The resistance of (TBA)0.3FeSe as a function of temperature, showing the superconducting transition. Reprinted with permission from ref. 151. Copyright 2018, IOP Science Publishing. (c) Schematic structure for the MoTe2 with organic-cation intercalation. (d) Sheet resistance of MoTe2 with organic-cation intercalation as a function of temperature; the inset shows the temperature-dependent resistance of MoTe2. (e) is the zoom-in view of (d) around the superconducting transition, showing the onset temperature of 7 K. Reprinted with permission from ref. 154. Copyright 2020, Elsevier. 
In addition to the layered 2D materials being intercalated with organic molecules to form vertical PNS that give rise to emergent properties as discussed above, two different kinds of 2D materials can be intercalated to form vertically stacked superlattices. Unlike oxide superlattices with strongly bonded heterointerfaces, which place a stringent requirement of lattice mismatch for the epitaxial growth, the weak van der Waals type interlayer coupling entails that 2D intercalated superlattices can be constructed arbitrarily by tolerating a much larger lattice mis-match or even incommensurate lattices. The so-called misfit layered compounds is such kind of 2D vertical superlattices with a general formula of (MX)1+α(TX2)m, in which the TMD layer with a m number of layers (represented by TX2) is incommensurately intercalated with a monochalcogenide layer (MX). α represents the mismatch index with the value of 0.08–0.28, which is related to the ratio of the mismatched lattice parameters.160 Among the numerous forms of misfit-layered compounds, those involving TaS2 have received particular attention because of a multitude of correlated states displayed by TaS2, including CDW, chiral charge order and superconducting transition.161,162 Early studies in 1990s demonstrated that the numerous types of (MS)1+αTaS2 misfit-layered compounds show the superconducting state with Tc above 70 mK.163 More recently, Sankar et al. reported a method to grow large-sized single crystal misfit compound of (SnS)1.15TaS2 through chemical vapor transport method.164Fig. 26a and b depict the HRTEM image and schematic view of the misfit (SnS)1.15TaS2, which confirms the quality of the intercalation. Transport measurements revealed the onset of the superconducting transition temperature of 3.01 K under ambient pressure (Fig. 26c). It is higher than the one of the pristine of TaS2 (Tc = 1.90 K)165 and other similar types of misfit compounds,165,166 indicating the enhancement of superconductivity. Additionally, the Tc shows a linear increase with the pressure, as shown in Fig. 26d, reaching 3.85 K at 1.95 GPa. This is likely due to the change of Fermi surfaces upon compression.167 In another example, large-size single crystals of (SnSe)1.16(NbSe2) misfit-layered compound have been reported as a multi-band superconductor with a Tc of 3.4 K.168 Both of these two examples suggest that the misfit PNS is an important system for studying the superconductivity in layered van der Waals materials.
 Fig. 26  (a) HRTEM image of the (SnS)1.15TaS2 superlattices from the cross-section. (b) The schematic of the (SnS)1.15TaS2 superlattices along the b-axis with the incommensurability shown in the SnS layer. Ta, Sn and S atoms are shown in red, blue and green, respectively. (c) Electricity transport measurements for the (SnS)1.15TaS2 superlattices for the in-plane and cross-plane directions. The inset is the zoom-in view of the superconducting transition period near 3 K. (d) Tc as a function of pressure. Reprinted with permission from ref. 164. Copyright 2018, American Chemical Society. 
Superlattices involving the highly anisotropic 2D materials also provide an avenue to explore superconductivity in its clean 2D limit, which can shed light on understanding the unconventional superconductivity or facilitate the construction of unconventional superconducting phases.169,170 Although it is possible to preserve the 2D superconductivity in a monolayer of 2D materials, it often requires encapsulation layers such as BN to protect the fragile 2D superconductivity from the external environment, which complicate the device fabrication and could introduce additional defects and/or disorders during the manual stacking of layers. Very recently, Kaxiras et al. developed a method to synthesize a novel 2D vertical superlattice consisting of alternating layers of a 2D superconductor H-NbS2 and a spacer layer Ba3NbS5, as shown in the STEM result (Fig. 27a).171 The individual layers of Nb H-NbS2 in the resulting Ba6Nb11S28 bulk single crystal are well decoupled and protected by the blocking layer with an electron mobility a few orders of magnitude higher than that in bulk NbS2 or unprotected monolayers. Unlike the misfit superlattices discussed above with incommensurate in-plane unit cells, the Ba6Nb11S28 superlattice has commensurate in-plane superstructures. The superlattice crystal turns into a superconducting state with an onset Tc of 1.6 K (Fig. 27b). The 2D superconductivity described by the BKT transition is revealed by the evolution of the power-law behavior in the temperature-dependent V–I curves (Fig. 27c) from a linear dependence close to the superconducting onset temperature to cubic dependence at the 2D BKT transition temperature of 0.82 K. Magnetotransport measurements show that the upper critical magnetic field, which destroys the superconductivity, increases rapidly as the magnetic field is tilted away from the direction normal to the 2D layers (Fig. 27d). This further corroborates the occurrence of a clean 2D superconducting state in the superlattice.
 Fig. 27  (a) HAADF-STEM image of Ba6Nb11S28 along the [100] direction with the simulated structures overlaid with one unit cell shaded in the transparent green box. (b) The resistivity of Ba6Nb11S28 as a function of temperature, showing the superconducting transition. The inset depicts a magnified view of the transition in temperature-dependent resistivity and magnetic susceptibility measured with zero-field cooling and field cooling, respectively. (c) V–I measurements from 0.28 K to 0.9 K. The inset shows the evolution of the power law V ∝ Iα. The dashed line represents α = 3. (d) Longitudinal resistivity as a function of the magnetic field for different values of θ. θ is defined as the angle between the surface normal and the magnetic field direction. Reprinted with permission from ref. 171. Copyright 2020, AAAS Publishing. 
The beauty of the bulk van der Waals superlattices described above lies not only in providing bulk crystalline materials with emergent or enhanced superconductivity, it also opens up a route to construct artificial quantum materials by incorporating 2D materials with other exotic quantum phases, such as topological insulators, which can be modulated or protected by the intercalated spacer layer. Individual flakes could be exfoliated from the bulk superlattice crystals, providing a cleaner and facile route towards 2D heterostructures without the need of manual stacking.


3.2.3. Superconductivity in moiré superlattices of twisted bilayer graphene. 
2D materials not only can be constructed into artificial vertical heterostructures and superlattices by introducing an arbitrary angle of rotation between two adjacent layers, such as in a bilayer graphene, a moiré superlattice with in-plane periodicity featuring a much larger supercell can be formed. The new periodic potential associated with the moiré pattern modifies the original band structure and electron transport behaviors, leading to many fascinating new properties, including unconventional superconductivity. It also gives rise to a burgeoning field called twistronics, which has quickly become the holy grail in condensed matter physics. Bilayer graphene, which normally adopts two graphene layers stacked in the Bernal (AB) stacking geometry, is the archetypical example of twisted moiré superlattices. By twisting the bilayer graphene system with a certain angle, the Dirac bands of graphene are modified into moiré Bloch bands that depend sensitively on the twisting angle.172 At a large twisting angle (e.g. θ > 10°), only the higher energy bands of the widely separated Dirac cones are modified by the interlayer coupling to form saddling points with corresponding Van Hove singularities (VHSs) in the DOS. The low energy bands close to the Dirac points remain barely changed from isolated graphene. As the twisting angle is reduced, interlay coupling starts to affect the low energy Dirac cone states with the VHSs brought closer to the Dirac point. Near the so-called magic angle (θ ≈ 1.1°), the VHSs merge into very narrow and ultra-flat moiré minibands (5–10 meV) with a nearly vanishing Fermi velocity, and the corresponding electron density is strongly localized at the AA stacking region of the moiré superlattice (Fig. 28a and b). The very low kinetic energy of electrons that fill the flat bands implies that the Coulomb interactions can dominate the electronic behavior, and drive the system into strongly correlated phases. Early experimental attempts at twisting angles close to the magic angle indeed revealed signs of correlated insulating states.173,174 A breakthrough was made in 2018 by Cao et al., who reported two ground-breaking discoveries in magic-angle bilayer graphene superlattices that exhibited Mott-like insulating phase at half flat-band filling (i.e., two electrons/holes per moiré unit cell)175 and most surprisingly, unconventional superconductivity when the system is electrostatically doped slightly away from the half-filling state.30 The group achieved the magic twisting angle in bilayer graphene by adopting a modified tear and stack method starting from a single monolayer graphene,173,175,176 allowing for the precise control of the twisting angle with a resolution of 0.1°–0.2°. The twisted bilayer graphene (TBG) was sandwiched between h-BN layers for encapsulation with a gate underneath the bottom h-BN that tunes the electron density in graphene (see Fig. 28c for device schematics). When the Fermi energy is moved below the charge neutrality point near the half filling of the lower flat band through electrostatic gating, the magic-angle TBG exhibits a superconducting transition with a Tc around 1 K (Fig. 28d) with highly anisotropic critical magnetic fields that destroy superconductivity. The temperature-dependent V–I characteristics confirm the 2D superconductivity with a BKT transition temperature of 1.0 K (Fig. 28e). Fig. 28f shows the heat-map for the four-probe resistance of one of the TBG devices (M1) as a function of density and temperature. Superconducting domes were noticed on each side of the half-filling Mott insulating state when the system is doped with additional holes or electrons (relative to half-filling). This phase diagram closely resembles that of high-temperature superconductors, such as cuprates, but is fully electrically tunable. This offers a great advantage to explore unconventional superconductivity and other strongly correlated phenomena over the entire competing phases without the need of introducing impurities or changing compositions, as in cuprates. Following Cao's work, Yankowitz et al. reproduced the superconductivity in TBG with a twist angle of 1.1° both in the hole-doped and electron-doped regions, and TBG with a twist angle larger than the magic angle can be brought back to the superconducting states by enhancing the interlayer coupling through pressure.177 Codecido et al. demonstrated that the TBG showed the superconducting phenomena at an even smaller twist angle of 0.93°, with a critical temperature of 0.3–0.5 K,178 and superconductivity with an even smaller twist angle of 0.79° was reported very recently;179 however, without adjacent Mott-like insulating state. These studies suggest that the range of the “magic” angle is more robust than what was previously believed, which, while it could benefit device applications, also places constraints on the theoretical explanations on the origin of the superconductivity. Nevertheless, moiré superlattices based on bilayer graphene provide a 2D carbon-based material platform to investigate the electron correlation with precise tunability, which in conjunction with band topology can lead to other exotic phases, such as ferromagnetism,180 quantum anomalous Hall (QAH) effect,181,182 and ferroelectricity.183 Following this success, twisting other 2D materials to realize moiré superlattices, such as topological materials, has become a fertile land to achieve the emergent superconducting state. For example, for WSe2, it has been reported that the zero resistance was observed at the cryogenic regime in twisted homo-bilayer WSe2, suggesting a possible transition to the full superconducting state.184
 Fig. 28  (a) The band structure of TBG in the first mini Brillouin zone of the superlattice at θ = 1.05°. (b) The DOS corresponding to the bands in (a), for energies of −10 meV to +10 meV. (c) Schematic of the TBG Hall bar device with gate architecture. (d) Four-probe resistance of two magic angle TBG devices (M1 and M2) as a function of temperature; the inset shows the optical image of device M1. (e) and (d) I–V curves for device M2 with density of −1.44 × 1012 cm−2 at different temperatures. (f) Four-probe resistance measured at different densities and temperatures for device M2. 3.3. Electronic and optoelectronic properties
Enhancing or tuning the electronic properties of materials always plays an essential role in material science. A lot of studies have been reported based on experimental and theoretical evidences that the materials’ band structures can be engineered by inducing the periodic structured nanomaterials, resulting in the modulation of the electronic properties in terms of the band gaps, mobilities, charge transport, and other properties.
It is well known that the energy band of PNSs consisting of two materials with different bandgaps can be tailored to have a narrow conduction miniband.185Fig. 29a gives the calculated miniband structure for the GaAs–Al0.3Ga0.7As superlattice with a well width of 6.5 nm and barrier width of 2.5 nm,186 in which the barrier width is small. The different approaches of the carrier transport in this special band structure, including the miniband transport,187,188 Wannier–Stark hopping189 and sequential tunneling,34,190 have been intensively studied.186 The strategy of introducing a periodic nanostructure to modulate the optoelectrical properties has been applied to a variety of semiconductor materials.
 Fig. 29  (a) The calculated miniband structure for the GaAs–Al0.3Ga0.7As superlattice with a well width of 6.5 nm and barrier width of 2.5 nm. Reprinted with permission from ref. 186. Copyright 2002, Elsevier. Magnified view of the band structures near the Γ point for (b) (InSb)n/InSb0.5As1.5 with n of (I) 1, (II) 2, and (III) 3, and for (c) (InAs)m/InSb0.5As0.5 with m of (IV) 1, (V) 2, and (VI) 3. Reprinted with permission from ref. 191. Copyright 2019, AIP Publishing. (d) Schematics of the molecular structure of (I) MAPbI3, (II) MASnI3, (III) sandwiched architectures of MAPbI3–MASnI3–MAPbI3, (IV) (MAPbI3)2–(MASnI3)2–(MAPbI3)2, and (V) (MASnI3)2–(MAPbI3)2–(MASnI3)2. Electronic properties of the (e) electrical conductivity per unit relaxation time (σ/τ), and the (f) electron contribution to the thermal conductivity (κe) along the three principal orthogonal directions (x, y and z) for the five structures (structures I, II, III, IV and V are represented in green, blue, red, black and pink curves, respectively). The variations with the chemical potential of the transverse components (along x and y directions) and longitudinal components (along z direction) are shown by (i), (ii) and (iii) respectively, in the figure. Reprinted with permission from ref. 193. Copyright 2017, The Royal Society of Chemistry. (g) Constant energy contours for the Au(111) surface with a superposed scheme of the Brillion zones, with the high-symmetry points obtained by integrating the photoemission spectral weight over a small energy window (±20 meV) with respect to the binding energy of 0 meV, 300 meV, and 600 meV, respectively. S1–S6 and S1′–S6′ represent the sp band folded into the Brillion zones of the 4 × 4 superlattice. Reprinted with permission from ref. 23. Copyright 2018, American Chemical Society. 
Atanu et al. systematically calculated the band structures of superlattice heterostructures of (InSb)n/InSb0.5As1.5 and (InAs)m/InSb0.5As0.5 with different thicknesses of the semiconductor segments.191 It was reported that an increase in the InSb layer thickness (n) in the (InSb)n/InSb0.5As1.5 structure could modify the band structure and change the number of carrier pockets. Fig. 29b and c demonstrates the band structures near the Γ point of heterostructures with different segment thicknesses. When the InSb layer thickness n was increased from 2 to 3, the band structure was changed from four band transitions with equal gaps of 0.115 eV to two indirect bandgaps of 0.054 eV; the number of carrier pockets in the band structure was reduced to one. In contrast, the change of the InAs thickness in the (InAs)m/InSb0.5As0.5 structure did not show a significant effect on the band structure, which indicated that the choice of a semiconductor segment in the superlattice dominates the effect of band engineering. Wei et al. characterized the properties of the superlattice InSe nanosheets, of which a periodic lattice distortion was induced by thermal annealing.192 The superstructure phase was stable in β-phase without transforming to In2Se3 or oxidizing to InSe1−xOx. Similar to the 2D In2Se3 superstructure,56 the superlattice InSe nanosheets exhibited metallic properties with a low current on/off ratio of 2.9. The electronic properties of the superlattice phase were significantly improved compared with the pristine InSe nanosheets. The carrier sheet density was increased from 0.996 × 1012 cm−2 to 6.37 × 1012 cm−2. The carrier mobility experienced great enhancement from 63.5 cm2 V−1 s−1 to 299.1 cm2 V−1 s−1.
Rahul et al. investigated the electronic transport through the superlattices by introducing periodic structures to perovskite structures for the synthesis of energetically stable geometries.193 Their periodic-structure perovskites had sandwiched architectures of MAPbI3–MASnI3–MAPbI3, (MAPbI3)2–(MASnI3)2–(MAPbI3)2 and (MASnI3)2–(MAPbI3)2–(MASnI3)2, as shown in Fig. 29d. The computational and experimental results revealed direct bandgaps of the superlattice perovskites, which was within the bandgap range of pure MAPbI3 and MASnI3 perovskites, and decreased with the addition of Sn-based perovskite to the structure. Both first principle computations and electrical resistivity measurement suggested that the periodic-structure perovskites were less electrically conductive, resulting from 103 times-lower δ/τ (the electrical conductivity per unit relaxation time) and κe (the electronic contribution of the thermal conductivity) than the typical perovskites along the longitudinal directions (Fig. 29e), even though the transverse components showed higher κe and δ/τ (Fig. 29f). Nevertheless, the κe of the superlattice perovskites was significantly reduced, which was contributed from the interface-induced impedances to thermal conduction and the energy scattering within each layer and across interfaces. This work provides a new strategy for the band engineering of perovskite materials through nanostructuring.
Furthermore, novel periodic structured nanomaterials, such as intercalated compounds or strain-engineered structures, can enhance the electronic and optoelectronic performance, which opens up the door to optoelectronic applications. The deposition of blue phosphorene on the Au(111) substrate was reported to form a periodic structure with large tensile strain, which strongly influenced the carrier dynamic.194 Jincheng et al. investigated this periodic nanostructure and electronic properties by angle-resolved photoemission spectroscopy (ARPES).23 The results revealed that the periodic potential induces the folding of the sp band in Au(111) (Fig. 29g). By combining ARPES with DFT simulations, it was found that the tensile strain residing in the 4 × 4 superlattice led to a reduced bandgap (∼1.1 eV), reduced electron effective masses and enhanced carrier mobility, compared with black phosphorus.
3.4. Optical properties
The formation of PNSs provides an approach to modify the optical properties of materials, such as the photonic band gap, light scattering, and plasmonic resonance. It enhances the intrinsic optical properties compared to their bulk counterparts, which pave a way for the next-generation photodetectors.
Photonic crystals present periodic dielectric structures, through which the light propagation has been well studied. 1D, 2D and 3D photonic crystals with the periodicity of dielectric materials along one or more directions exhibit interesting light phenomena. The device applications of the photonic crystal can be designed by combining simple optical components of mirrors, waveguide and resonant cavities based on theoretical predictions of optical properties.195 Here, examples of integrating the periodic nanostructures to modulate light propagations and optical properties in specific devices are discussed.
In 2011, Li et al. demonstrated the tunable photonic bandgap in one-dimensional superconducting-dielectric photonic crystal superlattices by a transfer-matrix method.196 With the swelling of the thicknesses of the superconductor layer and the dielectric layer, an enlarged width of the forbidden gaps and increased number of photonic bandgaps can be achieved. Some PNSs showed temperature-sensitive refractive indexes, which provided an easy-to-manipulate method to tune the optical properties of such materials. Ye et al. reported a systematic analysis to address this temperature effect on the optical properties of lithium niobate-based and liquid crystal-based 1D superlattices.197 From theoretical calculations, they found that the band structures and the density of states (DOSs), as well as the propagation wavelength, the refractive index and layer width were tunable by varying temperature. Especially, the concurrence of the null photonic gap and zero refractive index occurred in the visible optical range via temperature modulation. For example, the null photonic gap and zero refractive index appeared simultaneously in the 450–664 nm range when increasing the temperature from 68 K to 29893 K. This work shows the potential of superlattice materials for adaptive photonic devices with temperature-adjustable optical properties.
Not only can introducing periodic molecular structures into the material tune the material properties, the rational design of a material system integrated with period structures can improve the overall properties as well. Yan and coworkers developed a light-trapping structure of 1D nanograting bonded porous 2D photonic crystal (PC) perovskite (G-PC-PVK) films, as illustrated in Fig. 30a.198 The regular periodic structure consisted of highly ordered nanogratings on a compact MAPbI3 perovskite layer with period 2D porous PCs. Benefiting from the porous scaffold and the nanoimprinting process, the as-synthesized G-PC-PVK films showed improved crystallinity, larger grain size and prolonged carrier lifetime, compared with conventional flat perovskite (F-PVK) films. In addition, owing to the coupling effect of grating diffraction and 2D PC architecture, the light reflectance and transmission were reduced, thus exhibiting enhanced absorbance (Fig. 30b and c), which led to improving the performance of their G-PC-PVK-based photodetectors. The 1D nanograting arrays also equipped the devices with polarization-dependent light detection with a peak-to-valley ratio of 1.6, indicating the high polarization sensitivity.
 Fig. 30  (a) Schematic diagrams of the fabrication procedure of the G-PC-PVK photodetector, and the collective advantages of the hierarchical light-trapping architecture. (b) Reflectance and (c) absorbance spectra of the perovskite films with different structures. Reprinted with permission from ref. 198. Copyright 2019, John Wiley and Sons. (d) Simulated reflectivity spectra of the InAs/GaSb type-II superlattice-based mid-infrared photodetector devices with different silver grating periods. Reprinted with permission from ref. 199. Copyright 2019, AIP Publishing. 
Similarly, Meng et al. applied the periodic plasmonic grating structure with the InAs/GaSb type-II superlattice active layer to mid-infrared photodetector devices.199 Silver gratings were embedded on the active layer to improve the light absorption. The integration of InAs/GaSb type-II superlattices and silver gratings showed a combination of the Fabry–Pérot mode and the surface plasmon polariton (SPP) waveguide mode. The gold electrode worked as a transmission-block layer to drive the back-and-forth light traveling, leading to the Fabry–Pérot cavity mode at a certain wavelength where the resonance was strong with the reflectivity of nearly zero. By controlling the thickness of the InAs/GaSb active layer, the reflectivity in the resonant absorption spectra could be tuned. At the same time, the period of the silver gratings influenced the SPP resonant wavelength. This hybrid mode greatly improved the light absorption at selective wavelengths without sacrificing the infrared absorption of their reference device without silver gratings. Their device with silver grating also exhibited high polarized sensitivity.
A fundamental understanding of light-matter interactions is critical to the rational design of periodic nanostructures for optical property modifications. The light interactions with periodic particle and hole arrays were comprehensively reviewed and summarized by FJG De Abajo in 2007.200 This work gives a quantitative model, which describes the light scattering in the planar periodic systems. One important topic in the research field of light-matter interactions is the nanophotonics in 2D and van der Waals materials. Polaritons in van der Waals materials, especially the highly confined plasmon-polaritons observed in graphene, have broadened the applications for sensing,201 nanolasering202 and light modulations,203 manipulating light under the nanoscale. Tailoring the material structure enables the tunable polaritonic characteristics of van der Waals materials towards desired optical properties. Polaritons in 2D and van der Waals materials have been systematically discussed in a number of reviews.204,205 Advanced technologies control photonic quasiparticles, such as plasmons, excitons and magnons, in the same way as photons. A representative review demonstrates a unified view of describing different light–matter interaction phenomena with photonic quasiparticles by macroscopic quantum electrodynamics, providing experimental directions.206
4. Applications
PNSs have been widely studied for various applications, such as energy conversion, optoelectronic and electronic devices. In the following sections, the applications of PNSs in water splitting, CO2 reduction, fuel cells, thermoelectricity, solar cells, photodetector, light-emitting diodes, and memory devices (Table 3) are constructively discussed.

4.1. Energy conversion
Energy is one of the key factors that promote the development of human society. However, the application of traditional fossil fuels caused a series of environmental problems that affect our lives. Therefore, the modern energy conversion has become a main strategy to produce clean energy for the sustainable development of society with the protection of nature. Different types of materials were developed for energy conversion,4,9,10,12 among which PNSs have demonstrated outstanding merits for the enhancement of energy conversion performance due to their unique structural periodicity. The periodicity of PNSs has introduced various benefits to PNSs for energy conversion, such as synergistic effects that can be enhanced to improve catalytic activity,207 catalysts-reactants nanospace interfaces that can be engineered to smoothen product transference,136,208 and bandgaps that can be modulated to broaden light absorption,209 to name a few. The detailed applications of PNSs in various energy conversion processes, including water splitting, CO2 reduction, fuel cells, thermoelectricity, and solar cells, will be discussed in the following sections.

4.1.1. Water splitting (HER, OER). 
Facing challenges of a global warming crisis, “carbon neutral” is one of the most important solutions that the scientific community has devoted to explore. However, reducing the emission of carbonaceous species is the first challenge. As one of the most potential clean energy candidates, hydrogen has attracted great attention as an ideal replacement for the fossil fuels since it has zero carbonaceous species emission. However, the efficient generation of hydrogen is one of the key problems impeding their practical applications. Water, one of the main sources of hydrogen, is cheap, easy to get, and abundant around the world. The most common hydrogen generation process by water is known as water splitting, which consists of two half reactions: (i) hydrogen evolution reaction (HER) and (ii) oxygen evolution reaction (OER).210–213 Due to the adverse issues during the water splitting process, extra energy is required to initiate and maintain the catalysis process. Therefore, various strategies including electrocatalytic,214,215 photocatalytic,216,217 and photoelectrocatalytic218,219 water splitting processes have been studied so far. Although catalysts based on precious metals, such as Pt, Ir, and their oxides, are good candidates for these strategies, their expensive prices are not ideal for large-scale production. Hence, catalysts that contain less or no precious metals are required to be designed for efficient water splitting. In the next sections, the catalytic activity of PNSs in water splitting will discussed.
4.1.1.1. Electrocatalytic water splitting. 
As an important branch of water splitting, electrocatalytic water splitting has been intensively studied. Based on the unique electronic properties, many electrocatalysts with PNSs were developed to optimize the cathodic HER and/or anodic OER, as outlined in Table 3.214,215
4.1.1.1.1. Electrocatalytic hydrogen evolution reaction. 
The electrocatalytic HER process involves a simple two-electron migration mechanism, whereas the reaction pathways are different, owing to the type of catalysts and pH conditions.215 According to the mechanism of electrocatalytic HER (Fig. 31), many catalysts were developed, aiming to reach or even outperform the catalytic activity of Pt. Catalysts with PNSs showed good performance in electrocatalytic HER,20,220 making them potential candidates for practical applications.
 Fig. 31  Reaction pathways of HER in acidic (in red) and alkaline (in blue) solutions. 
2D materials, such as 2D TMDs,221 graphene and its derivatives,222,223 are well studied for water splitting. For instance, metallic 1T-MoS2 nanosheets (NSs) showed good electrocatalytic HER activities with Tafel slopes of ∼40 mV dec−1,224,225 where graphene acts as either electroactive component or functionalized support.223,226 Constructing layer-by-layer PNSs using different 2D materials may significantly enhance the catalytic activity via synergistic effect.207 A van der Waals heterostructure consisting of g-C3N4 and graphene is one such example for electrocatalytic HER.227 Qiao et al. used a 2D porous g-C3N4 nanolayer that was exfoliated from porous C3N4 and N-doped graphene to construct PNSs (denoted as PCN/NG). The out-of-plane pores between the layers of PCN/NG can benefit mass transport during the catalysis process, where the in-plane pores can provide sufficient active sites for catalysis. Therefore, PCN/NG exhibited good electrocatalytic HER activity with a lower Tafel slope of 49.1 mV dec−1 after initial 750 cyclic voltammetry (CV) compared with the corresponding value of PCN/NG without CV cycles (Tafel slope = 88.6 mV dec−1).27 Another example is the unilamellar metallic nanosheets, which exhibit better electronic property in energy conversion application in comparison to the corresponding few-layered bulk material. Sasaki et al. restacked unilamellar metallic 1T phase MoS2 nanosheets and cationic poly(diallyldimethylammonium chloride) (PDDA)-modified reduced graphene oxide (PDDA-rGO) to construct 1T-MoS2/PDDA-rGO superlattice for electrocatalytic HER study. Benefitting from the loosely restacked structure of the 1T-MoS2/PDDA-rGO superlattice, a large interlayer spacing of ∼2.5 nm was expected to provide more accessible active sites to enhance the electrocatalytic HER performance, offering a low overpotential of 137 mV (current density = 10 mA cm−2) and Tafel slope of 48.6 mV dec−1.28 Although the 1T phase MoS2 has good catalytic activity, the 2H phase MoS2 is a thermodynamically stable structure. Ma and co-authors prepared a periodic nanostructure based on 2H-MoS2 and unilaminar graphitic nanocarbon (2H-MoS2/GNC). Different from the direct assembly of two materials, the synthesis of 2H-MoS2/GNC experienced a calcination process after threonic acid intercalation into MoS2 (T-MoS2). The interlayer spacing of 2H MoS2 increased from 0.63 nm to 0.99 nm in T-MoS2, decreasing both the overpotential and Tafel slope from over 500 to 421 mV and 103 to 53 mV dec−1, respectively. However, a slight decrease in the interlayer spacing from 0.99 to 0.98 nm was observed after the formation of 2H-MoS2/GNC, whereas its overpotential and Tafel slope significantly reduced to from 421 to 195 mV and from 53 to 47 mV dec−1, respectively. The enhanced electrocatalytic HER activity of 2H-MoS2/GNC may be ascribed to the conducting unilaminar graphitic nanocarbon structure that promoted an efficient electron transfer for the catalysis and shortened diffusion distance.228
Confined catalysis has attracted increasing attention due to the nanoconfinement effect, which can be used to enhance the catalytic activities in some reactions, such as CO oxidation, H2 oxidation, and electrolysis of water.229 MoS2 has demonstrated good HER activity in acidic condition, owing to the proper adsorption energy of hydrogen at its edge.230 Hence, 2D MoS2 confined nanoparticle electrocatalysts may exhibit high catalytic activity. Various precious metal nanoparticles, including Pt, Ru, Pd, and Au nanoparticles, were intercalated into the layers of MoS2. All of them showed good catalytic activities towards HER, especially the MoS2-confined Pt nanoparticles (MoS2/Pt-NPs) exhibited superior catalytic activity and long-term stability, which outperformed the commercial Pt/C catalysts. The flaw of the conventional way of modifying metal catalysts on the surface of 2D materials is that the metal catalysts would corrode in acidic condition and leach away, which can be avoided by the MoS2/Pt-NP system due to the synergistic host-intercalant effect.80 In comparison to the high HER activity of MoS2 in acidic condition, MoS2 suffered from poor HER activity in alkaline condition due to the sluggish water dissociation process.231 However, it is still important to study alkaline HER since the cost of alkaline electrolytes can be decreased, and the alkaline condition allows the use of earth-abundant transition metals to further reduce the cost compared to that of the precious metals-based catalysts.215 Co species, such as Co(OH)2, is widely considered as good water dissociation centres since it can reduce the energy barrier to break the H-O bond by absorbing oxygen, which can benefit the water dissociation in alkaline conditions. Therefore, Liu and co-authors introduced Co(OH)2 nanoparticles to multilayered MoS2 nanosheets to construct a 2D MoS2 confined Co(OH)2 nanoparticle (MoS2/Co-NP) electrocatalyst. The new MoS2/Co-NP catalyst enhanced the HER performance via accelerating water dissociation in alkaline condition with a Tafel slope of 53 mV dec−1.81
The moiré superlattice has experienced a rapid development in recent years due to the unconventional superconductivity at the magic-angle in twisted bilayer graphene.30,175 The conductivity of the moiré superlattice can be easily tuned by adjusting its twisted angle. Conventional methods to enhance the catalytic activity of MoS2 mainly focused on optimizing popular factors, such as active sites and vacancies. However, the interlayer potential barrier is seldom studied. Yuan and co-authors have fabricated a MoS2 moiré superlattice with a twisted angle of θ ≈ 7.3°. Benefitting from the twisted structure of the bilayer MoS2, a reduced interlayer potential barrier was confirmed by first-principles calculations and ultralow frequency Raman spectra. The low interlayer potential barrier promotes the electron transfer from the conductive substrate to the active sites in the moiré superlattice of MoS2, leading to an enhanced electrocatalytic HER performance with a Tafel slope of 73 mV dec−1.133
Wang et al. utilized a template-assisted printing-based assembly process to construct striped-pattern (SP) superlattices. The SP superlattices could catalyze various gas evolution reactions by using various components. The small gaps between the strips of the SP superlattices can provide a smaller bubble stretch force and higher transference, which significantly enhanced the catalytic activity (Fig. 32). For instance, Pt nanoparticles-based SP superlattices showed a significant enhanced electrochemical HER performance with small gap widths between the strips of 5 μm, yielding a small Tafel slope of 33 mV dec−1 and an overpotential of 15 mV.136
 Fig. 32  (a) Schematic illustration of the growth of gas bubbles on a flat film electrode, which caused a large number of inactive sites to form. (b) Significant stability difference between the flat film and SP electrode. Reprinted with permission from ref. 136. Copyright 2020, American Chemical Society. 
4.1.1.1.2. Electrocatalytic oxygen evolution reaction. 
Different from the simple two-electron transfer HER process, OER involves a complex four-electron proton-coupled reaction (Fig. 33), which requires higher energy to overcome the sluggish kinetics.232
 Fig. 33  The OER mechanism for acid (blue line) and alkaline (red line) conditions. Reprinted with permission from ref. 232. Copyright 2016, the Royal Society of Chemistry. 
Due to the good electric conductivity, layered structures, such as layered metal oxides, including metal hydroxides (M(OH)2, where M refers to transition metals) and metal oxyhydroxides (MOOH) showed high electrocatalytic OER activity.232 In particular, early 3d transition metal-based hydr(oxy)oxides, benefitting from their low overpotential in alkaline conditions, have attracted great attention for constructing cost-effective OER electrocatalysts.233 Furthermore, their layered double hydroxides (LDHs) showed comparable electrocatalytic efficiency to noble metals (such as IrO2).234
The PNSs constructed by layered metal oxides and other 2D layered materials can further enhance the electrocatalytic OER activity via synergistic effect.235 Graphene and its derivatives are good candidates to be the counterparts to construct multilayered PNSs with LDHs, since their good electrical conductivity,223 high mechanical strength,236 and large surface area237 provide good support to LDHs in catalysis.238 Ma et al. utilized NiMn239 and CoNi240 LDHs to construct layered PNSs with graphene oxide (GO) or reduced graphene oxide (rGO) for electrocatalytic OER. The NiMn/GO and NiMn/rGO PNSs were the first studies. Compared to the thickness of GO (∼1.0 nm), rGO nanosheets have a thinner thickness of ∼0.5 nm due to the oxygen groups having been partially removed. The difference in the thickness between GO and rGO induced the various interlayer spacings of NiMn/GO (∼1.1 nm) and NiMn/rGO (∼0.83 nm). Since the close distance between the layers can shorten the path and accelerate the charge transfer, NiMn/rGO showed better electrocatalytic OER activity with a smaller overpotential of 0.26 V and Tafel slope of 46 mV dec−1, where the corresponding values of NiMn/GO are 0.28 V and 50 mV dec−1 in alkaline condition (1 M KOH aqueous solution), respectively.239 Co(OH)2 has been widely used for catalysis. However, its inferior electrical conductivity is not satisfactory for being an electrocatalyst. Hence, different morphologies, atomic substitutions, and counterparts with high conductivity may significantly enhance the electrocatalytic activity of Co(OH)2. Layered CoNi hydroxides, with various ratios of Co and Ni (Co1−xNix, where x = 0, 1/2, 1/3, 1/4, 1), and GO-based PNSs (Co1−xNix/GO) were studied for electrocatalytic OER. The Co2/3Ni1/3/GO showed excellent electrocatalytic OER activity with the overpotential of 259 mV and a Tafel slope of 35.7 mV dec−1, which indicated that the rate-determining step is the transformation of M-O to M-OOH. The outstanding catalytic activity may be ascribed to the composition tunability, which optimizes the electronic structure and binding intermediates and catalysts.240 However, the catalytic activity of the CoNi-based catalyst may be improved by using rGO as a counterpart rather than GO, according to the previous study.239 To further develop the catalysts, there are various strategies, such as changing components of PNSs109 and exposing more active sites of catalysts.241 Despite the graphene and related materials having good properties for electrocatalysis, their corrosion in alkaline condition cannot be ignored.242 Therefore, the more robust and carbon-free RuO2.1, with high catalytic activity and low redox potential was used to replace the role of GO and rGO. Recently, the trimetallic CoNiFe LDHs and RuO2.1 nanosheets were used to assemble CoNiFe/RuO2.1 PNSs. Compared to the mono or bimetallic LDHs, trimetallic catalysts offered better OER catalytic activity via synergistic effect.243 Hence, the CoNiFe/RuO2.1 yielded an overpotential of 281 mV and a Tafel slope of 48.9 mV dec−1.109 An increase of the exposure of active sites is a general strategy to improve the catalytic activity. Since the edges of LDHs are important sources of active sites for catalysis, the array of small LDH nanosheets can offer more active sites than the large LDH nanosheets with the same mass. Zhao et al. prepared periodic tiled/standing nanostructures (NiAl/GO) based on NiAl LDH and GO. The electrocatalytic activities were studied based on the different ratios of NiAl:GO (1:1–5:1 for NiAl/GO-1, 5.6:1 for NiAl/GO-2, 6:1 for NiAl/GO-3, 7:1 for NiAl/GO-4, and 8:1–10:1 for NiAl/GO-5, respectively), where NiAl/GO-5 showed the best electrocatalytic OER activity in 0.1 KOH aqueous solution with a Tafel slope of 118 mV dec−1, and low overpotential of 200 mV (current density of 100 mA cm−2) and 180 mV (current density of 50 mA cm−2), respectively (Fig. 34). The good electrocatalytic activity of NiAl/GO-5 may be ascribed to: (1) the high ratio of NiAl:GO that provides more active sites; (2) the standing form of NiAl nanosheets that exposes more active sites, as compared with the tiling form.241 However, replacing the NiAl LDHs with other more active materials may yield enhanced catalytic activity.
 Fig. 34  The preparation process of NiAl/GO materials, and the assembled structures of (a) NiAl/GO-1, (b) NiAl/GO-2, (c) NiAl/GO-3, (d) NiAl/GO-4, and (e) NiAl/GO-5, respectively. Reprinted with permission from ref. 241. Copyright 2019, Springer Publishing. 
The layer-by-layer method is a more controllable process to prepare PNSs with an accurate layer number, as required. Iron porphyrin (Fe-PP) is a good candidate for enhancing the charge transfer between LDHs and the electrode due to its extensive 2D 18 p-electrons. Therefore, periodic (CoNi/Fe-PP)n (where n refers to the bilayer number) ultrathin films (UTFs) were prepared based on CoNi LDHs and Fe-PP via electrostatic layer-by-layer technique. The thickness of the (CoNi/Fe-PP)n UTF increased by ∼2 nm per bilayer number, and its OER catalytic activity was continuously enhanced until the bilayer number n increased to 30, with the lowest overpotential of 264 mV and Tafel slope of 37.6 mV dec−1 in 1.0 M KOH. Then, the corresponding activity decreased as a result of the excess bilayer number.104 The results indicate that the proper thickness of the periodic catalyst is necessary for efficient OER catalysis, and an excess thickness may hinder the charge transfer and mass diffusion, thereby influencing the catalysis activity.
4.1.1.1.3. Electrocatalytic overall water splitting. 
Electrocatalytic overall water splitting (OWS) is the integration of electrocatalytic HER and OER.244 Therefore, the catalysts for electrocatalytic OWS need to be rationally designed for both HER and OER. There are two possible strategies that can apply to develop PNSs to catalyze OWS: (1) assemble dispersed catalysts for OWS in good order to enhance the catalysis activity; (2) integrate various catalysts for HER and OER, respectively, into hetero PNSs to realize electrocatalytic OWS.
Featured with high surface area and abundant active sites, the well-ordered and close-packed nanoparticle-based 3D superlattices have attracted much attention for the first strategy to efficiently catalyze OWS. Nitrogen-doped carbon (CN)-coated CoMnO nanoparticles were studied as units for constructing CoMnO@CN nanoparticle superlattices. Herein, CoMnO cores offered an optimized electronic structure for electrocatalytic OER, where the CN shells showed good electronic structure for HER and enhanced electrical conductivity between neighboring nanoparticles. Therefore, the collective properties of the CoMnO@CN superlattices showed good electrocatalytic OWS with low overpotentials of 263 and 71 mV at 20 mA cm−2 for OER and HER, respectively.37
A general strategy to fulfill the requirements for OWS is to utilize the collective properties of PNSs constructed with various materials with either electrocatalytic HER or OER activity. Ma et al. combined NiFe LDHs and GO/rGO for constructing Ni2/3Fe1/3/GO and Ni2/3Fe1/3/rGO, respectively, for water splitting. With the assistance of GO, Ni2/3Fe1/3/GO achieved good electrocatalytic OER activity with an overpotential of 0.23 V and a Tafel slope of 42 mV dec−1. However, the Ni2/3Fe1/3/rGO demonstrated a slightly improved OER activity with a lower overpotential of 0.21 V and a smaller Tafel slope of 40 mV dec−1, which can be ascribed to the better conductivity of rGO. In addition, the observed HER activity of Ni2/3Fe1/3/rGO indicated its ability for FWS catalysis.245 However, rGO is not an ideal candidate for HER catalysis, whereas TMDs, especially MoS2, showed excellent HER activity. In addition, DFT calculations predicted an enhanced charge transfer between the interlayer of LDH and TMD nanosheets, which may further improve the electrocatalytic activity. Hence, various PNSs based on NiAl, NiFe, and MoS2 were synthesized as area-balanced (NiAl/MoS2-1 and NiFe/MoS2-1), charge-balanced (NiAl/MoS2-2 and NiFe/MoS2-2), and LDH-excess (NiAl/MoS2-3 and NiFe/MoS2-3) compositions, respectively. The charge-balanced PNSs, NiAl/MoS2-2 (overpotential of 0.22 V and Tafel slope of 67 mV dec−1 for HER, overpotential of 0.31 V and Tafel slope of 56 mV dec−1 for OER) and NiFe/MoS2-2 (overpotential of 0.18 V and Tafel slope of 82 mV dec−1 for HER, overpotential of 0.25 V and Tafel slope of 45 mV dec−1 for OER), showed the best electrocatalytic activities for both HER and OER. According to X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), increased binding energies for Ni 2p, Fe 2p, and Al 2p, and a decreased binding energy for Mo 3d were observed. The oxidation of Ni, Fe, and Al ions were accompanied with a reduction of the Mo ions, demonstrating the charge transfer from LDHs to MoS2, which verified the prediction by DFT calculations.29 A recent study further revealed the importance of materials selection for building periodic nanostructure-based catalysts. Three different restacking PNSs, MoS2/NiFe, NiFe/Graphene, and MoS2/Graphene, were prepared. The NiFe/Graphene showed improved OER activity (Fig. 35a), and MoS2/Graphene showed enhanced HER activity (Fig. 35b) compared to sole NiFe and MoS2, respectively, due to the graphene-accelerated charge transfer for both NiFe/Graphene and MoS2/Graphene to further increase their catalytic activity. However, due to the significantly enhanced synergistic effect, MoS2/NiFe showed the best catalytic activity for both HER and OER with an overpotential of 0.11 V and Tafel slope of 77 mV dec−1 for HER, and an overpotential of 0.21 V and Tafel slope of 46 mV dec−1, indicating its high catalytic activity towards OWS (Fig. 35c).107
 Fig. 35  Schematic of the (a) NiFe/Graphene superlattice for OER, (b) MoS2/Graphene superlattice for HER, and (c) MoS2/NiFe superlattice for the overall water splitting. Reprinted with permission from ref. 107. Copyright 2019, American Chemical Society. 
4.1.1.2. Photocatalytic water splitting. 
Photocatalytic water splitting is one of the general methods to directly transfer solar energy to chemical fuels. The photocatalysis process includes multiple parameters, such as photon absorption, exciton separation, carrier diffusion, carrier transport, catalytic efficiency, and mass transfer of reactants and products. Each parameter contains detailed factors that will influence the catalytic activity (Fig. 36).216 Hence, various catalysts were designed to optimize the photocatalytic water splitting process,217,246,247 and in particular, the PNSs are good candidates since they have unique electronic and optical properties.
 Fig. 36  Schematic illustration of the parameters associated with photocatalysis. Reprinted with permission from ref. 216. Copyright 2017, American Chemical Society. 
4.1.1.2.1. Photocatalytic hydrogen evolution reaction. 
Photocatalytic HER yields from water splitting have three key steps: (1) photon absorption; (2) exciton separation and migration; (3) surface reaction and gas desorption. Various catalysts were designed to optimize these steps to further enhance the photocatalytic HER activity.248
Compared to the dense materials, porous semiconductors showed advantages for photocatalysis since the porous structure reduces the charge recombination rate of hole-electron pairs. This is due to the shortened distance between the reactive surface and the place where the pairs are generated. In addition, the cavities of the porous structure can accommodate other components to further improve their photocatalytic activity via synergistic effect. Therefore, Feng et al. synthesized a series of crystalline porous chalcogenide semiconductors based on In3+, S2−, and one of the transition metal cations (such as Cd2+, Zn2+, Mn2+, and Cu+). ICF-5InCuS-Na (Na14In17Cu3S35·xH2O), with a 3D diamond-type superlattice structure with a low band gap of 2.0 eV, is suitable for photocatalysis in the visible light region. ICF-5InCuS-Na showed good HER activity with 18 μmol h−1 g−1 of H2 gas production in Na2S aqueous solution under visible light irradiation, which can be maintained for over 96 h with the accumulation of more than 890 μmol of H2.249 Subsequently, Hu et al. synthesized a series of soluble supertetrahedral chalcogenido T4 clusters (T4-1, T4-2, and T4-3) with twisted diamond-type superlattice structures. T4-1 ((BMMim)9(Cd3In17S31Cl4), where BMMim refers to 1-butyl-2,3-dimethylimidazolium) and T4-3 (BMMim)9(Cd3In17Se31Cl4)(4,4′-bpy), showed a twisted hexagonal diamond-type superlattice structure, whereas T4-2 ((BMMim)9(Cd3In17Se18Cl4)) showed a twisted cubic diamond-type superlattice structure. Compared to the band gaps of T4-1 (3.25 eV) and T4-2 (2.76 eV), the lowest band gap of 2.60 eV for T4-3 offered T4-3 the best photocatalytic activity with the H2 evolution rate of 15.9 μmol h−1 g−1 in the solid state and 91.5 μmol h−1 g−1 in DMSO, whereas corresponding values of T4-1 and T4-2 are 3.5 and 5.6 μmol h−1 g−1 in the solid state, and 6.1 and 27.5 μmol h−1 g−1 in DMSO (Fig. 37), respectively. Replacement of S with Se offered weak bond energy for the H-bonds and adjusted band structure, which enhanced the photocatalytic activity of the T4 clusters. However, besides the different components, the poor solubility of T4-1 limited further enhancement of the photocatalytic activity in DMSO, which also exhibited the importance to introduce Se to replace S for enhanced solubility.250
 Fig. 37  (a) Photoluminescence spectrum of 5 mg of T4-3 dispersed in 20 mL DMSO. (b) Possible charge transfer process of T4 clusters dispersed in solution. (c) Recycling photocatalytic HER test under visible light illumination on T4 clusters dispersed in DMSO. Reprinted with permission from ref. 250. Copyright 2019, American Chemical Society. 
Song et al. prepared highly stable 1T-MoS2 in aqueous solution via the intercalation of ammonium ions (denoted as N-MoS2). N-MoS2 showed a zigzag chain superlattice structure, which disappeared when transferred to 2H-MoS2via 350 °C annealed treatment. N-MoS2 was then hybridized with CdS nanorods, the suitable light absorber, for photocatalytic H2 production. N-MoS2/CdS showed outstanding photocatalytic HER activity with a H2 evolution rate of 9.11 mmol g−1 h−1, which is 21-fold of the corresponding value for 2H-MoS2/CdS. The significant difference between the photocatalytic activity of N-MoS2/CdS and 2H-MoS2/CdS can be ascribed to: (1) the photogenerated electrons that can be transferred to metallic N-MoS2 rapidly for proton reduction reaction, which is more efficient in comparison to the semiconducting 2H-MoS2; (2) the metallic phase of N-MoS2 may offer more active sites for HER than the semiconducting 2H-MoS2.251
4.1.1.2.2. Photocatalytic oxygen evolution reaction. 
As a part of the photocatalytic water splitting process, photocatalytic OER has attracted intensive study targeting catalytic efficiency enhancement. Different catalysts were prepared to optimize the catalysis mechanism since OER involves a more complex four-electron transfer than HER.
Benefitting from the good photocatalytic activities of ZnCr LDH and titanate, charge-balanced PNSs of ZnCr/titanate were prepared by Hwang et al. The electron transfer between ZnCr LDH and titanate induced a spatial separation of holes and electrons, which further decreased the rate of electron–hole recombination (Fig. 38). Both strong electronic coupling between the layers of two semiconducting materials and the expansion of the surface area contributed to the improvement of the photocatalytic OER activity of ZnCr/titanate with an O2 gas production rate of 1.18 mmol h−1 g−1, which is almost 2-fold higher than that of pristine ZnCr LDH (0.67 mmol h−1 g−1).252
 Fig. 38  (a) Cross-sectional high resolution TEM image of the ZnCr/titanate periodic nanostructure, and (b) its enlarged view and structural model. (c) PL intensity of ZnCr/Titanate with different titanate:ZnCr ratios of 0.91 (-), 1.16 (---), 1.46 (-·-·-), pristine ZnCr LDH (○), and protonated layered titanate (△). (d) Schematic model for the band structures of the ZnCr/titanate periodic nanostructure. Reprinted with permission from ref. 252. Copyright 2011, American Chemical Society. 
4.1.1.2.3. Photocatalytic overall water splitting. 
Different from photocatalytic HER and OER that have already been studied by experiments, studies on the photocatalytic OWS mainly stay at the theoretical calculations. However, these calculation results have demonstrated the great potential of the periodic nanostructure for photocatalytic OWS.
Various PNSs for photocatalytic OWS were studied via DFT and related calculation methods, regardless of the dimensions of materials. For instance, calculations on semiconducting GaN/ZnO superlattice nanowires demonstrated an enhanced visible-light absorption due to its narrowed bandgap, which can be ascribed to the formation of intermediate bands within the bandgaps, referring to the potential of GaN/ZnO for being photocatalysts for OWS.209 The calculations on Ag2ZnSn(S1−xSex)4 (x = 0–1),253 2D hydrogenated-silicene/halogenated-silicene superlattices (SHSXSLs, X = F, Cl, Br, and I),254 and 2D InFeO3255 showed either improved charge mobility253 or enhanced charge separation.254,255 Whereas the key factor that influenced their potential for photocatalytic OWS is their periodic structures that offer modulated bandgaps for the absorption of visible light.209,253–255 The vacancy of experimental research in photocatalytic OWS based on PNSs offered great opportunities for future systematic studies since the theoretical studies have already indicated its direction.


4.1.2. CO2 reduction. 
The excess emission of carbon dioxide (CO2) since the industrial revolution via combustion of fossil fuels has become a significant environmental problem because the high concentration of CO2 induces a critical greenhouse effect. Besides the elimination of fossil fuels, the reduction CO2 to CO is a strategy for both renewable energy and carbon neutrality. However, the high dissociation energy of ∼750 kJ mol−1 for the CO bond is a key barrier before the CO2 reduction reaction (CRR). Hence, various catalysts were designed with different solutions to overcome the barriers of CRR.256,257
Au–Cu bimetallic systems showed attractive electrocatalytic CRR activity, which is controlled by the ratio of each composition.258 Besides the ratio of each composition in the bimetallic system, the order of the structure also showed its unique contribution towards electrocatalytic activity by modulation of active site exposure and mass transfer.208 The electrocatalytic activity between the ordered and disordered materials can be significantly different. For instance, the disordered alloy AuCu bimetallic nanoparticles mainly produce H2 gas, whereas after the transformation from a disordered structure to ordered structure with alternate layers of Au and Cu, the ordered AuCu bimetallic nanoparticles (Fig. 39) showed significantly enhanced CRR activity with a high faradaic efficiency of ∼80%, a lower overpotential of ∼200 mV and an enhanced catalytic turnover by 3.2-fold.259
 Fig. 39  (a and b) Aberration-corrected HAADF-STEM images of AuCu (a and b) and (c and d) magnified STEM images of the center of the particle together with a schematics illustration of the structure projections. Reprinted with permission from ref. 259. Copyright 2017, American Chemical Society. 
The aforementioned method for SP superlattice preparation has provided an attractive platform for different types of catalysis by tuning its composition.136 When replacing Pt with a Co porphyrin (CoTPP), a metal–organic complex showed potential for CRR,260 and with GO at a mass concentration ratio of 2:1, the new CoTPP SP superlattice showed good CRR activity with a higher faradaic efficiency of ∼70% than CoTPP films (∼55%). The convexo-concave structure of the CoTPP SP superlattice accelerates the diffusion of CO2 to further increase the concentration of reactant CO2 and decrease the competitive reactant H2O on the electrode simultaneously, yielding a good selectivity between CRR and HER.136


4.1.3. Fuel cells. 
A fuel cell is a chemical device that directly transfers chemical potential energy stored in fuels to electrical energy with high efficiency, and consists of a cathodic oxygen reduction reaction261,262 and anodic fuel oxidation reaction.263,264 During the reaction, fuel cells need a continuous supply of fuel and oxygen to maintain the chemical reaction. For better catalytic performances, many different materials were studied for electrodes for fuel cells, with good electronic properties. Periodic nanostructured materials are among the choices.
4.1.3.1. Oxygen reduction reaction (ORR). 
Oxygen reduction reaction is one of the most important parts for fuel cells. The most desirable pathway for the oxygen reduction reaction is the reduction of oxygen to water, which involves a direct 4-electron pathway. However, the sluggish cathodic ORR kinetics limit the performance of fuel cells. Therefore, various catalysts for accelerating the ORR process were designed and synthesized, aiming to further enhance the efficiency of fuel cells.
