Pt-Based catalysts are widely studied for electrocatalytic ORR due to their extraordinary catalytic activity and stability, whereas their high cost and scarcity limit their practical applications. Hence, many efforts were devoted to developing new Pt-based catalysts for enhancing activity and stability, and minimizing the portion of Pt to reduce cost.261 It was reported that the presence of Pd could significantly promote the catalytic activity of Pt towards ORR by synergistic effect. This is ascribed to the modification of the Pt d-band centre by Pd through diminishing the electronic interaction between the exposed Pt and oxygenated species, and thereby improving ORR activity. The Pt and Pd nanocrystals-based 3D binary superlattices (BNSLs) showed superior electrocatalytic ORR activity compared to the Pt octahedra and Pt–Pd random mixture.262 However, the use of alloy nanoparticles to construct superlattice arrays (SLAs) is the more general strategy. Wang et al. produced PtAu SLAs by using bimetallic PtAu nanoparticles. In comparison to the commercial Pt/C (50%, wt.%) and Pt black catalysts, the new PtAu SLAs exhibited enhanced electrocatalytic ORR activity with higher catalytic current density and more positive onset potential (0.903 V).264 However, the alloys based on two precious metals still suffer the limitation of high production cost, which may be solved by the introduction of earth-abundant metals to alloys. Hence, trimetallic PtCoFe alloy-based monolayer superlattices were prepared. This 2D PtCoFe superlattice displayed a high electrochemically active surface area (ESCA) of 62.9 m2 g−1, which is comparable with 70.2 m2 g−1, the ESCA of commercial Pt/C. The PtCoFe monolayer superlattice showed excellent electrocatalytic ORR activity with high specific activity and mass activity, which are 17.4- and 15.2-fold higher than the corresponding values of Pt/C (Fig. 40).265 Although these alloy-based superlattices have already demonstrated high electrocatalytic ORR activity, their catalytic activities can still be further enhanced via ordering the composition of alloy nanoparticles.208 For instance, structural ordered bimetallic PtFe266 and trimetallic PtNiPb267 alloy nanoparticles showed enhanced electrocatalytic ORR activities.
 Fig. 40  (a) Schematic representation of the formation process of the trimetallic PtCoFe nanoparticle superlattices. (b) CVs recorded at room temperature in 0.1 M HClO4 solution with the sweep rate of 50 mV s−1. (c) ORR polarization curve recorded at room temperature in an oxygen-saturated 0.1 M HClO4 aqueous solution with a sweep rate of 10 mV s−1 and a rotation rate of 1600 rpm. (d) Mass activity and specific activity at 0.9 versus RHE. (d) ORR polarization curves before and after ADTs. Reprinted with permission from ref. 265. Copyright 2017, John Wiley and Sons. 
The development of a Pt-free catalyst for electrocatalytic ORR is the ultimate strategy to decrease the cost for their practical applications. Manganese oxides are potential precious metal free electrocatalysts for ORR. Especially Mn3O4, benefiting from its mixed valence of Mn, exhibits rich electrochemical properties. Therefore, carbon-coated tubular monolayer superlattices (TMSLs) were constructed by using hollow Mn3O4 nanocrystals (denote as h-Mn3O4-TMSLs). The structure of h-Mn3O4-TMSLs offered fast mass transport efficiency and enhanced the exposure of active sites. In addition, the carbon coating further enhanced the electron transport and structural robustness. With all these benefits, h-Mn3O4-TMSLs showed excellent electrocatalytic ORR activity with a positive onset potential of ∼0.91 V and diffusion-limiting current density of ∼5.7 mA cm−2, which are comparable to the corresponding values of Pt/C (∼0.90 V and ∼5.8 mA cm−2).268 Featured with large surface areas and highly exposed active sites, 2D materials have always been good candidates for various catalysts, especially since their catalytic activity can be further enhanced via building 2D/2D PNSs due to the synergistic effect. The incorporation of C3N4 nanosheets, Fe clusters and Ti3C2 nanosheets yielded a 2D/2D periodic nanostructure of FeNC/TiC. FeNC/TiC provided a large accessible surface area of 30 m2 g−1, and showed good electrocatalytic ORR activity with a small onset overpotential of ∼0.92 V, which is similar to the Pt/C benchmark.269
4.1.3.2. Ethanol oxidation reaction (EOR). 
Ethanol is a common liquid with low toxicity and low production cost. In comparison to compressed hydrogen, ethanol is more convenient for storage. With all these benefits, the ethanol-based fuel cell has attracted much attention from the scientific community. However, the process of the ethanol oxidation reaction is kinetically more difficult than that of the hydrogen oxidation reaction. Hence, various catalysts were developed to enhance the efficiency of the ethanol oxidation reaction.263,270–273
Although the precious metal-based catalysts for EOR have many limitations for large-scale applications, several precious metal-based catalysts were developed due to their extraordinary catalytic activity.270–273 Constructing alloys by using precious and non-precious metals is a general method that can be applied to decrease the production cost and enhance the catalytic activity. The aforementioned trimetallic PtCoFe alloy monolayer superlattices showed bifunctional catalytic activity for both ORR and EOR. This catalyst exhibited a peak current density of 4.75 mA cm−2 and high mass activity, which are 17.1 and 3.8 times higher than commercial Pt/C benchmark, respectively.265 Like their ORR activity, the EOR activity of the PtCoFe alloy monolayer superlattices may also be enhanced via ordering of the PtCoFe nanoparticles.208
4.1.3.3. Methanol oxidation reaction (MOR). 
Methanol is another candidate for fuel cells. Methanol offers many advantages in fuel cells in comparison to ethanol, such as low CO2 emissions, and easier to catalyze since there is no C–C bond in methanol to break. In addition, methanol can be easily synthesized from natural gas and from other renewable biomasses. Hence, intensive studies focused on the development of new catalysts have emerged for optimizing the MOR process.
Besides the composition, the structure of the catalyst also influences the ultimate catalytic activities. An ordered array of active metal alloy nanoparticles can demonstrate enhanced catalytic activity.208 For instance, the PtAu superlattice array showed good electrocatalytic MOR activity with a peak current density of 41.77 mA cm−2, which is significantly higher than that of commercial Pt/C (13.91 mA cm−2),264 whereas the study of the PtPd nanocrystal superlattices further confirmed the importance of the formation of PNSs for catalytic activity. Compared to the monodispersed PtPd nanocrystal nanoparticles, PtPd nanocrystal superlattices showed extraordinary MOR activity with ECSA of 46 m2 g−1 and specific kinetic current density of 2.08 mA cm−2.274 Crack-tips enriched Pt–Cu superlattice nanoflakes (NFs) were employed as highly efficient anode electrocatalysts for MOR in methanol fuel cells. The Pt–Cu NFs showed 4 times higher mass activity, 6 times higher specific activity and 6 times higher stability than those of the commercial Pt/C catalyst due to the synergistic effects of “crack-tips” and “superlattice crystals”.275


4.1.4. Thermoelectricity. 
With the increasing concern on the environmental protection situation, the invention and development of clean energy has become a key area of global scientific research, among which the thermoelectric conversion technology has been considered an efficient renewable energy technology due to its small size, high reliability, no emission of pollutants, and wide operating temperature range. Thermoelectric materials have the capacity to reversibly convert thermal energy to electric. The thermoelectric materials that combine n- and p- type materials use a temperature difference to generate electricity, and utilize electricity to accurately control temperature. The efficiency of a thermoelectric material is determined by the dimensionless figure of merit ZT = S2σT/κ, where σ is the electrical conductivity, S is the Seebeck coefficient, T is the absolute temperature, and κ is the thermal conductivity and κ can be expressed with κp+ κe (for the phonon and the electronic contribution, respectively). To compete with other energy technologies, ZT is expected to be above 3, but this has not been realized so far. It seems that the simple strategy of increasing the power factor S2σ and decreasing κ can improve the ZT value. However, those values often counteract each other. For example, the increasing of the electrical conductivity normally causes a decline of S and rise of κe. Therefore, it is essential to optimize those parameters to achieve high ZT.
Nanoengineering has achieved many fruits to improve ZT by means of boundary scattering at the interface, where the thermal conductivity decreases without affecting the power factor too much. Recently, periodical nanostructures show much power to control those parameters through filtering low-energy carriers and magnifying the electron density of the states to increase the Seebeck coefficient, adjusting the carrier concentration and mobility to improve the electrical conductivity, and coherent phonon effects to further reduce the thermal conductivity at the sites of boundary scattering. In the following, we will summarize recent achievements on those periodical nanostructures of thermoelectrical materials.
In 2002, a substrate-free, bulk-like PbSeTe/PbTe quantum dot superlattice was fabricated by molecular beam epitaxy and a cooling device based on one-leg of this material was set up. A recorded ZT value of 2.0 was obtained even without optimized structure parameters, and this device cooled down to 43.7 K below room temperature (300 K).68 Lately, the ZT values higher than 4 and 6 are predicted for 5 nm PbSe/PbS and PbTe/PbSe superlattice nanowires at 77 K, respectively, by optimizing parameters such as the segment length, diameter, materials, and doping level. This work stated that the quantum confinement in the superlattice nanowires induced the potential barrier–well inversion as the electron mass differences between different materials.276 A ballistic thermoelectric transport was proposed in a superlattice nanowire. Because of the low temperature, dephasing from electron–electron and electron–phonon scattering can be ignored, resulting in higher output power than a 3D superlattice material.277 However, for practical applications, we may need high superlattice nanowire density, where interwire transport needs to be considered, such as electron hopping strengths, energy levels, and electron tunneling rates.278 Those discoveries inspired the investigation of superlattices with other materials.
As an eco-friendly light-element-based thermoelectric material, C/Si/Ge superlattices are studied both theoretically and experimentally. The thermal conductivity was predicted to be decreased by 92% in Ge/Si superlattice nanowires compared to pure Si nanowires due to the interface phonon scattering and coherent effect.279 By optimizing the wire diameter and superlattice period, ZT values above 1 was proposed for 1.7 nm Ge/Si superlattice nanowires because of the enhanced Seebeck coefficient, unique electronic properties, and ultralow lattice thermal conductivity.280 Further doped Ge/SixGe1−x superlattices exhibited enhanced ZT and power factor compared with both SiGe alloy and Si/Ge superlattice materials at room temperature because of the doping that increased the electrical conductivity.281 The optimized doping concentration in the SixGe1−x barrier was calculated within the range of 10–20%.282 When combined with graphene, the atomically thin C3Si3/C (C3Ge3/C) superlattice could achieve a ZT value of 1.95 (2.72) by tuning the carrier concentration.283 If a conducting polymer was alternately assembled with the layered SiGe alloy, the thermal conductivity was reduced much more than the electrical conductivity, resulting in a doubled ZT value.284 The decrease of the thermal conductivity on n-Ge/SixGe1−x superlattices was experimentally observed.69,285,286 The doping concentration and strain in Si1−xGex help to increase the Seebeck coefficients by tuning the energy band structure and neutralizing the negative interface effect on the electrical conductivity.69,286
For high temperature applications, the Al2O3 interlayer in the Al2O3–ZnO (AO/ZnO) superlattice thin films was fabricated by atomic layer deposition.287 An enhancement of the Seebeck coefficient was observed due to the quantum confinement, whereas the in-plane thermal conductivity was reduced by 261 times compared to the bulk materials, despite the decrease of electrical conductivity. The overall ZT value was doubled compared with the AO/ZnO film.287 In terms of the cross-plane, the low energy carrier filtering and quantum confinement clearly enhance the power factor, producing ZT above 0.45 at 500 K after being combined with low thermal conductivity. This value is of 26 times improvement compared with the ZnO film.288 During the studies, new methods to measure all cross-plane thermal electric properties in superlattice films were developed, where a micro-Peltier heater and Cu foil/plates were used.289,290 They discovered that the Seebeck coefficients are not only related to the temperature, but also varied with the applied temperature difference.289 Furthermore, 6-cycled AO/ZnO superlattice films were tested with the highest ZT value of ∼0.45 at 500 K.290 After that, they applied this test method to measure the Seebeck coefficient, electrical conductivity, and thermal conductivity of the p-type Bi2Te3/Bi0.5Sb1.5Te3 superlattice film, where a recorded ZT value of 1.44 was obtained.291 The Hf-doped ZnO superlattice films were also prepared with atomic layer deposition to obtain a net improvement in the power factor. Further introduction of 18O into the Hf:ZnO films suppressed the thermal conductivity without scarifying the electrical properties.292
Other oxides are also fabricated into superlattices. In [(NbOx)a/(Nb-doped SrTiO3)b]20 superlattices, a decrease in the layer thickness ratio (a/b ratio) would improve both Seebeck coefficient and electrical conductivity.293 For YBa2Cu3O72–La2/3Ca1/3MnO3 superlattices, an interesting phenomenon was found, where its Seebeck coefficient is positive, while the two individual materials have negative Seebeck coefficients due to a long-range electronic reconstruction at the interfaces.294 The thermoelectric properties of a series of ABO3 compound superlattices were calculated with density functional theory and Boltzmann transport theory. The interface polarity at the LaNiO3/SrTiO3(001) superlattices leads to a band offset and the formation of an electric field, and then induced multiband splitting.295 A large positive Seebeck coefficient (135 μV K−1) and a high figure of merit (0.35) for room-temperature was predicted.295 In (LaNiO3)n/(LaAlO3)n(001) superlattices, high Seebeck coefficients of ±600 μV K−1 were predicted under tensile strain.296 The epitaxial strain could tune the metal-to-insulator transition and the band velocities near the Fermi energy. They found that the thinner layer with n = 1 strongly enhanced the thermoelectric properties compared with n = 3 because the confinement induced Ni-site disproportionation.296 A similar phenomenon was also found in (SrCrO3)1/(SrTiO3)1(001) and (SrMnO3)1/(SrTiO3)1(001) superlattices with Seebeck coefficients of 500–600 μV K−1 near the band edges.297
Another family of periodical thermoelectrical materials is the organic–inorganic superlattices, which has been reviewed by Karppinen et al. in 2015.298 Herein, we will state the advances of organic–inorganic superlattices after that. The main merit of this material is its flexibility and suitability for portable device fabrication. Usually, they are prepared by periodically combining the conductive polymer/organic molecules with other inorganic thermoelectrical materials, such as Bi2Se3/Bi2Te3, layered double hydroxides, TiS2 and others. There are different types of organic–inorganic superlattices. For example, Bi2Te3 nanoparticles are monodispersed in the continuous conductive polymer matrix. The periodic nanofillers significantly scatter phonons, while the continuously conducting polymer phase provides high electronic transport, and this resulted in an excellent ZT value at room temperature.299 Different ladder-type heteroacene molecules in 2D gold nanoparticle–molecule superlattices modified the energy levels to determine the electrical conductivity and affected the Seebeck coefficient by adjusting the charge carrier in the arrays, and thus adjusting the PF.300 The periodic assembly of the layered double hydroxide layer structure with poly(3,4-ethylenedioxythiophene)-poly(styrene sulfonate) produced an organic–inorganic alternately superlattice, in which the carrier mobility was improved to enhance the electrical conductivity. Furthermore, the low-energy carriers were filtered by sufficient interfaces between organic and inorganic phases, and thus promote the Seebeck coefficient, resulting in improved power factor.301
A general method was developed to synthesize flexible thermoelectric materials through a facile electrochemical intercalation.302 The organic cations intercalated into the TiS2 layers to form a hybrid superlattice of alternating inorganic TiS2 monolayers and hexylammonium ions (Fig. 41). The lattice thermal conductivity was reduced by two orders of magnitude due to stronger phonon scattering, and the electrical conductivity was increased due to the increased carrier density in the hybrid material. The electron mobility is not affected because the Coulomb force can be effectively screened by the polar water molecules and the positive hexylammonium ions. As a result, a high ZT of 0.28 at 373 K was obtained. Furthermore, the mechanical flexibility is even lower than that of plastic materials, such as Teflon. This method can be possibly used to fabricate other organic–inorganic superlattices using many other layered material candidates, such as Bi2Te3 or the transition metal dichalcogenides and various organic molecules with different functional groups.302 Inspired with the above work, the Bi2Se3/organic superlattice with enhanced ZT value was prepared with multiple steps, including lithium intercalation, ionic exchange, and organic exchange.303
 Fig. 41  Structural model of the TiS2 (a) single crystal and the (b) TiS2[(HA)0.08(H2O)0.22(DMSO)0.03] hybrid superlattice. (c) HAADF-STEM image of TiS2[(HA)x(H2O)y(DMSO)z]. Reprinted with permission from ref. 302. Copyright 2015, Springer Nature Publishing. 
For thermoelectrical applications with periodic nanostructures, many parameters need to be optimized, such as the period length, height, and shape of the periodic potential barriers.304 Other effects, such as the surface roughness304 and applied fields (e.g., magnetic field),305 may also play important roles in determining the thermoelectric properties. In addition, there are many more materials in this family, such as graphene/h-BN, Bi2Te3/WS2 and (LaS)1.2/CrS2,306,307 giving more and more novel combinations to new materials in the near future.


4.1.5. Solar cells. 
As one of the most important applications in energy conversion, solar cells have been developed for decades, and they are widely applied to various areas. Along with the development, different types of solar cells are also studied, such as III–V GaAs solar cells, quantum dots solar cells, dye-sensitized solar cells and perovskite solar cells, aiming at high solar power conversion efficiency (PCE) and stability for industrial manufacturing and real-life application. During the development of solar cells, PNSs contribute to their unique properties, leading to the improvement of solar cells, which will be summarized in this section.
4.1.5.1. GaAs based solar cells. 
The GaAs-based solar cell is one of the most important branches of solar cell studies. According to the report from National Renewable Energy Laboratory (NREL), GaAs solar cell has the top conversion rate,308 with strong plasticity, good temperature resistance, and good sensitivity to light. The PNSs offer better performance to this type of solar cell.
GaAs PNSs are intensively studied for the enhanced optoelectrical properties in comparison to their bulk counterparts.309 Lattice-matched InGaAsP/InGaP superlattices were grown with alternate layers of In0.32Ga0.68As60P40 well, and the In0.49Ga0.51P barriers were fabricated into solar cells with good performance (Fig. 42a).33 The EQE at wavelengths beyond the band-edge of In0.49Ga0.51P was greatly improved with increased periods, as shown in Fig. 42b. In this report, the carrier escape mechanism of the superlattice structure was studied. It was found that the carrier tunneling and thermionic emission coexisted in the system, while the carrier tunneling played the dominant role with a thin barrier, as indicated in Fig. 42a. However, for the strain-balanced InGaAsP/InGaP structure, the tunneling probability was zero due to a relatively thicker barrier.310 Under the coexistence of carrier tunneling and thermionic emission, the EQE was improved because carriers are more likely to escape from the quantum well (QW) region and promote the photocurrent.33 Their solar cell device showed further enhanced EQE at resonance peaks beyond the band-edge, with the Fabry–Pérot resonance induced by the gold back surface reflector. Resulting from the high EQE in the sub-bandgap, the JSC was improved by 26% compared with the standard InGaP solar cells (Fig. 42c). The VOC in one sun condition was 0.08 V higher than that of the bulk InGaAsP solar cells. The resultant JSC, VOC, FF, and PCE of the device with back surface reflector and antireflection coating were 20.5 mA cm−2, 1.136 V, 75.3%, and 17.6%, respectively. It also confirmed the positive effect of the back surface reflector and the antireflection coating on the device performance. However, the optimization on the lattice-matched superlattices are desired to increase the relatively low JSCJSC and FF. Recently, a single-junction p-i-n GaAs solar cell fabricated with low-cost flexible metal foils was reported.311 By optimizing the thickness of the intrinsic layer, the best device achieved a PCE of 13.2%, showing great opportunity for enhancing the device performance by modifying the GaAs layer.
 Fig. 42  (a) Schematic diagram of the structure of the lattice matched InGaAsP/InGaP superlattice-based solar cell, and the corresponding energy band diagram, illustrating tunneling and thermionic-emission carrier transports in this structure. (b) EQE beyond the band-edge of In0.49Ga0.51P (680 nm) versus the wavelength of the InGaAsP/InGaP superlattice solar cell with different numbers of periods. (c) IV curves of the 100-period device, with and without ARC, with a gold BSR and with a back-filter, under AM 1.5G illumination. Reprinted with permission from ref. 33. Copyright 2017, AIP Publishing. (d) Convergence of the carrier extraction efficiency with the number of SL periods for different period thicknesses at a photon energy of 1.25 eV and an effective carrier lifetime of 10 ps. The convergence is faster for the thicker-period device due to the stronger localization. (e) Converged carrier extraction efficiency as a function of the period thickness. The transition of the carrier extraction from quasi-ballistic to sequential tunneling resulted in a significant drop in the carrier extraction efficiency. Reprinted with permission from ref. 34. Copyright 2018 AIP Publishing. 
Fundamental studies on the carrier dynamics of periodic structure based-solar cells also revealed the great potential for performance enhancement. Aeberhard et al. performed a quantum-kinetic simulation to investigate the carrier transport in the GaAsSb/GaAsN type-II QW superlattice solar cells.34 It was reported that the electron and hole states were localized with increased period thickness, reflecting different carrier transportations with various period thicknesses. At the periodic thickness (dper) of 3 nm, photocarriers proceeded quasi-ballistically, which converted to sequential tunneling with a thermal component when the dper was increased to 12 nm. The transition of the carrier extraction from quasi-ballistic to sequential tunneling resulted in a significant drop in the carrier extraction efficiency (Fig. 42d and e). This work provided a theoretical background in optimizing the design of the GaAsSb/GaAsN superlattice towards efficient carrier extraction in solar cells.
4.1.5.2. Quantum dots solar cells. 
Quantum dots (QDs) are the sub-nanometre sized semiconducting nanoparticles, which show tunable electronic and optical properties through controlling the size of individual QDs or periodic structure of the QDs’ array due to the quantum mechanisms favored by solar cells. The parameters, such as the radius, dot-to-dot spacing and number of layers have a profound effect on the device performance.312 Computational simulations revealed that the PCEs of QD solar cells (QDSCs) are highly dependent on the structure of QDs. For Ge/Si QDSCs, QDs with a large volume have a stronger quantum confinement effect and lower ground-state energy, while a large thickness of QDs has a negative effect on the PCE. An optimum PCE of 27.22% was predicted for bilayer conical QD superlattices (QDSLs) with optimized size.
As mentioned, introducing PNSs could induce a miniband in the energy band, which significantly influences the carrier transport. The minibands could also form in closely-stacked QD through coupling the electronic states along the stacked directions.313 Sho and coworkers demonstrated that the miniband in InAs/GaAs QDSCs had the effect of spatially separating carriers in the built-in field, which subsequently increased the carrier lifetime and inter-subband absorption strength.314 In particular, the formation of a miniband significantly enhanced the two-step photocarrier generation. The temperature-dependent time-resolved-PL decay results revealed that a miniband was formed above 40 K. The photocurrent caused by the inter-subband excitation significantly increased with the formation of a miniband at temperatures above 40 K, which evidenced the effective carrier separation in the miniband. Lee et al. first reported a layer-by-layer method to fabricate distance-controlled CdTe QDSLs with modulated dimensions of the quantum resonance.315 Simply changing the concentration of the QD solution, the distances between the quantum dots were controlled, thus achieving the dimension modulation of 1D, 2D-monolayer and 3D-multilayer QD superlattices. Quantum resonance was achieved in 3D with high in-plane QD density, which means that the quantum resonance not only occurred in the stacking direction. Fig. 43a–d shows the excitation energy-dependent PL spectra of the CdTe solution and QD superlattices. Obviously, no PL peak shift was observed for the QD superlattices, while the peak positions shifted to lower energies with decreased excitation energy for the QD solution with no quantum resonance. This evidenced the formation of minibands in the CdTe QDSL with different dimensions. Introducing highly mismatched GaSb QD superlattices into bulk GaAs solar cells were illustrated to have the effect of preserving the VOC in the solar cells.316 By using the intermediate band material QD with a partially filled intermediate band, the sub-bandgap photon absorption was increased, which consequently enhanced the overall performance by 11.43% compared with the bulk single-junction GaAs solar cells.
 Fig. 43  Excitation energy dependence of the photoluminescence spectra of the (a) CdTe QD solution in which the quantum dots are randomly dispersed, and CdTe QDSLs exhibiting (b) 1D, (c) 2D, (d) 3D quantum resonances. The solid lines show PL spectra with different excitation energies with dashed lines to show the peak value. The excitation energy increases from top to bottom. The insets show the absorption spectra of each sample. The down arrows indicate the excitation energies in the PL measurements. Reprinted with permission from ref. 315. Copyright 2020, Springer Nature Publishing. (e) Schematic diagram of the InAs/GaAs QDSCs of five- and one-periods of InAs/GaAs QDSLs. (f) Excitation density-dependent JSC, (i) and VOC, (ii) at the excitation energy of 1.32 eV and temperature of 15 K. Blue and red circles represent the results of the devices with five- and one-periods superlattices, respectively. Reprinted with permission from ref. 319. Copyright 2018, Springer Nature Publishing. 
Tanibuchi and coworkers applied time-of-flight spectroscopy to investigate the carrier transport dynamics in InAs/GaAs QDSL.317 They observed a rapid carrier flow across the cell under the excitation with high power density, resulting from the state filling effect in the QDSCs. Under strong excitation, a large number of carriers were generated to fill the ground states. The carrier transport through the excited state miniband also occurred under the excitation above the critical power density, which accelerated the carrier transport. This work demonstrated a direct way to investigate the ultra-fast carrier dynamics of the QDSL with a probe structure in the quantum well structure, revealing fundamental knowledge. The studies on the carrier dynamics of the QDSL SCs address the importance of band engineering for efficient SCs, for which the band tuning effect of the periodic nanostructure is one of the strategies.
Hot carrier solar cells can utilize the excess energy of hot carriers to generate power, providing a great potential to increase the PCE. The low-dimensional periodic-nanostructure system has been demonstrated to have slowed carrier thermalization.318 The performance of hot-carrier QDSCs shows the dependence on the period of the periodic structures as well.319Fig. 43d illustrates the device structure of InAs/GaAs QDSL SCs with the superlattice period of 5 and 1. The light absorption of the device cell increased with a higher period, which subsequently improved the hot carrier extraction-induced VOC and JSC (Fig. 43e). The tunnel-assisted carrier escape is also suppressed in high-period QDSL as the adjacent superlattice re-traps the hot electrons, extracted to the conduction band edge.
4.1.5.3. Dye-sensitized solar cells. 
The dye-sensitized solar cell (DSSC) has been developed through mimicking photosynthesis in nature. Its easy-fabrication, low cost and high performance have attracted great research interests.320,321 The typical DSSCs consist of two primary reactions, which are photoexcitation of the dye by an oxidation reaction on photoanodes and the regeneration of the dye by a reduction reaction on counter electrodes, respectively. The counter electrode transfers electrons to the electrolyte for further process. Pt is the most commonly used material for counter electrodes in DSSCs. However, it is easy to be corroded and the cost is quite high.322 Periodic-nanostructured materials with unique properties are under the investigation for electrode materials. In 2014, Gratzel's group achieved a PCE of 13% with porphyrin dye-based dye-sensitized solar cell.323 Graphene nanoplatelets on FTO glass were employed as the counter electrode material. Wang et al. synthesized a superlattice counter electrode material with highly ordered P2Mo18/MoS2 superlattice nanosheets on C nanoparticles (P2Mo18/MoS2@C).324 The superlattice structure is depicted in Fig. 44a. A 1D interface was formed between the 2D nanosheet and the 3D nanoparticles, resulting in a large specific surface area of 174.2 m2g−1, providing more catalytic active sites. In this hierarchical structure, the electron transport kinetics improved. Cyclic voltammetry measurement and electrochemical Impedance spectroscopy revealed that the superlattice material showed prominent properties of high reduction peak current density, a low peak-to-peak separation and high catalytic capability and high diffusion rate of I3−, compared with Pt. A PCE of 8.85% was achieved by the DSSC with this material, while their Pt reference device demonstrated a PCE of 7.37% under AM 1.5G illumination (Fig. 44b).
 Fig. 44  (a) Schematic diagrams of the structure of C nanoparticles, P2Mo18/MoS2 superlattices and P2Mo18/MoS2@C. (b) IV curves of the dye-sensitized solar cell devices with different counter electrodes under AM 1.5G illumination. Reprinted with permission from ref. 324. Copyright 2019 American Chemical Society. 
4.1.5.4. Perovskite solar cells. 
Perovskite has attracted much attention over the last decade due to its superior optoelectrical properties. A record PCE of 25.5% has been achieved for single-junction perovskite solar cells, which is comparable to that of the silicon-based solar cells.308 A 2D perovskite with periodic quantum-well structure, which consists of alternately arranged metal halide layers and organic layers, exhibits better stability over the 3D perovskite materials, offering a great potential for solving the stability issue of the 3D counterparts.325 The Ruddlesden–Popper (RP) phase 2D perovskites have the general formula of A2A′n−1MnX3n+1, where the A site is occupied by a large alkylammonium cation, A′ represents the monovalent organic cation, M is occupied by a divalent metal cation, X is a halide, and n is the number of metal halide monolayer sheets between two neighboring organic layers. For the Dion-Jacobson (DJ) phase 2D perovskite, diammonium cations occupy the A site, forming the formula of AA′n−1MnX3n+1. By varying n, the thickness of each perovskite layer can be tuned.
In 2016, Hsinhan and coworkers adopted the hot-casting technique to fabricate perovskite solar cells based on the RP phase 2D mixed-cation (BA)2(MA)3Pb4I13 perovskite (n = 4) (BA: butylamine, MA: methylamine).326 The solar cell device with the structure of FTO/PEDOT:PSS/(BA)2(MA)3Pb4I13/PCBM/Al demonstrated a peak PCE of 12.52%, a VOC of 1.01 V, a JSC of 16.76 mA cm−2 and a FF of 74.13%. The 2D-based devices were far more stable than 3D solar cells. The unencapsulated 2D device experienced 20% of degradation in PCE after 200 hours of light illumination, while the 3D device only retained 40% of its original PCE after 24 hours. Their (BA)2(MA)2Pb3I10 perovskite (n = 3) device with the same device structure had a PCE of 11.44%, a VOC of 1.056 V, a JSC of 14.37 mA cm−2 and a FF of 75.39%, which was inferior to the (BA)2(MA)3Pb4I13 (n = 4) device because the insulating organic layer inhibits the carrier transport in the out-of-plane direction. Constantinos et al. successfully fabricated a (BA)2(MA)4Pb5I16 perovskite (n = 5)-based solar cell with the architecture of FTO/PEDOT:PSS/(BA)2(MA)4Pb5I16/PCBM/Al.22 The corresponding PCE achieved 8.71% with a VOC of 1.0 V, a JSC of 11.44 mA cm−2 and a FF of 75.59%. However, the mixed-cation perovskite solar cells were not comparable to pure 3D phase solar cells in PCE, mostly due to the large bandgap.
Generally, the optoelectrical properties of the RP-phase perovskite would be improved when increasing the n value from 1 to 4, but deterioration occurs with higher n values. The mixed phases with different n values would lead to the structure distortion and, consequently, poor film quality. The coexistence of 2D and quasi-3D phase were reported in NpMA (1-naphthalenemethylammonium) and AnMA (9-anthracenemethylammonium)-based A2(MA)2Pb4I13 perovskite films (n = 4).327 Diffraction rings and Bragg spots were both observed in the GIWAXS patterns (Fig. 45a), implying the disordered orientation. The existence of several phases was clearer in the HRTEM image, in which various lattice spacings were found in different regions (Fig. 45b).
 Fig. 45  (a) GIWAXS patterns of the NpMA-Pb film (left) and the AnMA-Pb film (right). (b) HRTEM images of the NpMA-Pb film (left) and the AnMA-Pb film (right); insets: Fast Fourier transforms (FFT) of the selected area diffraction. Reprinted with permission from ref. 327. Copyright 2020, American Chemical Society. (c) Schematic diagrams of the charge transport of MTEA (top) and BA-based (bottom) 2D perovskite films. (d) J–V curves of (MTEA)2(MA)4Pb5I16 and (BA)2(MA)4Pb5I16-based SCs under AM 1.5G illumination; inset: schematic diagram of the 2DRP perovskite-based solar cell. Reprinted with permission from ref. 328. Copyright 2020, Springer Nature Publishing. 
To enhance the interaction between the two adjacent inorganic frames, MTEA+ (MTEA: 2-(methylthio)ethylamine) was adopted as the spacer cation in the RP-phase 2D perovskite structure.328 As illustrated in Fig. 45c, an extra sulphur–sulphur interaction was formed between the two META+ ions in addition to the weak van der Waals force, which strengthened the interaction between the two inorganic layers and induced a highly ordered orientation, facilitating the carrier transport across layers. Distinct and discrete Bragg spots were observed in the GIWAXS pattern of (MTEA)2(MA)4Pb5I16 (n = 4) perovskite, suggesting the highly oriented crystal phase. Accordingly, a lower defect density and higher carrier mobility were characterized. A PCE of 18.06% was recorded for the device of ITO/PEDOT:PSS/(MTEA)2(MA)4Pb5I16/PC61BM/Cr/AU with a VOC of 1.088 V, a JSC of 21.77 mAcm−2 and a FF of 76.27% (Fig. 45d). The device maintained 87.1% of its initial PCE after 1000 hours of operation under AM 1.5G illumination in a glovebox.
In the RP phase 2D perovskite, the two adjacent organic layers interact with each other by van der Waals force, while in in the DJ phase, the relatively weak force is substituted by the hydrogen bonding between two amino groups, having the potential to reinforce the structure stability. Li et al. reported on an ultra-stable DJ phase (PDA)(MA)3Pb4I13 (PDA+: 1,3-propanediamine) 2D perovskite solar cell.31 The molecular structure is illustrated in Fig. 46a. The amino groups at both ends of the PDA2+ bound with the inorganic frame by hydrogen bonding. The optoelectrical properties of (PDA)(MA)n−1PbnI3n+1 with n = 1, 2, 3, 4 were characterized. With increased n values, a broader light absorption was observed (Fig. 46b), as the bandgaps were reduced from 2.46 eV for n = 1 to 1.63 eV for n = 4, suggesting a tunable bandgap. Solar cell devices with the structure of FTO/TiO2/(PDA)(MA)n−1PbnI3n+1/Spiro-OMeTAD/Au were fabricated. The PCE increased with the increase of n. The device with (PDA)(MA)3Pb4I13 had the best performance of a PCE of 13.3%, a VOC of 0.98 V, a JSC of 19.5 mA cm−2 and a FF of 69% (Fig. 46c), while the RP phase 2D (PA)2(MA)3Pb4I13 (PA+: propylamine) perovskite-based solar cell device displayed the highest PCE of only 8.8%. Through TRPL and EIS measurements, it was found that the carrier extraction of the DJ-based device at the FTO/TiO2 side was more efficient, and the charge recombination resistance is over two times larger than the RP-based device. The (PDA)(MA)3Pb4I13-based solar cell showed ultrahigh stability, which retained 95% of the original efficiency after the exposure to ambient air for 4000 hours (Fig. 46d) and to light illumination for 3000 hours. Shi et al. introduced asymmetric diammonium DMAPA (DMAPA: 3-(dimethylammonium)-1-propylammonium) into the perovskite structure and synthesized (DMAPA)MA3Pb4I13 with n = 4.329 The solar cell device with the structure of FTO/TiO2/(DMAPA)MA3Pb4I13/Spiro-OMeTAD/Au exhibited the performance of a PCE of 15.16%, a VOC of 1.06 V, a JSC of 22.07 mA cm−2 and a FF of 65% (Fig. 46e). 80% of its initial PCE was maintained after the 300 hours under AM 1.5G illumination in air. Bat-El reported on the DJ-phase perovskite with a high n value of 10, (BzDA)(Cs0.05MA0.15FA0.8)9Pb10(I0.93Br0.07)31 (BzDA:1,4-benzenedimethanamonium).330 BzDA consists of diammonium groups and an aromatic ring, which could stabilize the molecular structure into an arranged orientation, and benefit the carrier transport with free π-electrons. Intensity-modulated photocurrent and photovoltage spectroscopies (IMVS/IMPS) and transient photovoltage and photocurrent (TPV/TPC) measurement revealed that this 2D perovskite exceeded the 3D perovskite ((Cs0.05MA0.15FA0.8)Pb(I 0.93Br0.07)3) in its recombination lifetime, charge collection efficiency, carrier diffusion length and carrier extraction under bias, mostly contributed from the molecular structure of BzDA. The best performance of solar cell devices in a mesoporous configuration, which is illustrated in Fig. 46f, displayed a PCE of 15.6%, a VOC of 1.02 V, a JSC of 21.5 mA cm−2 and a FF of 71% (Fig. 46g). The 2D-based device exhibited better stability than the 3D-based device, which retained 80% of the initial PCE after 84 hours under the dark in an ambient environment.
 Fig. 46  (a) Schematic diagram of the RP and DJ Phase 2D Layered Perovskites. (b) UV-Vis spectra of (PDA)(MA)n−1Pbn+1I3n=1; inset: color variation of the films with the increased n values. (c) J–V curve of the device based on (PDA) (MA)3Pb4I13 under AM 1.5G illumination; inset: stabilized photocurrent measured at the maximum power point (Vmp = 0.72 V). (d) Stability test of different SCs in ambient air. Reprinted with permission from ref. 31. Copyright 2019, Elsevier. (e) J–V curves (forward and reverse scans) and related PV parameters for the ‘champion’ SC based on (DMAPA)MA3Pb4I13. Reprinted with permission from ref. 329. Copyright 2020, The Royal Society of Chemistry. (f) Schematic diagram of the perovskite SC based on (BzDA)(Cs0.05MA0.15FA0.8)9Pb10(I0.93Br0.07)31 and the organization of the perovskite layers on the substrate. (g) J–V curve of the best device based on (BzDA)(Cs0.05MA0.15FA0.8)9Pb10(I0.93Br0.07)31. Reprinted with permission from ref. 330. Copyright 2019, American Chemical Society. 
The 2D perovskite has demonstrated great potential for ultra-stable solar cells, but the enhancement in PCE is highly desired. Generally, 2D perovskites with n = 1 are not suitable for solar cell devices despite the high stability. This is because of the inefficient carrier transport limited by the strong quantum-well effect,331 while the high n values decrease the stability due to increased 3D compositions. So, the rational design of a perovskite structure with optimized n value should be considered. It is worth noting that during the synthesis, the phase purity is critical. A mixed structure with multiple n values would lead to low film crystallinity with low uniformity and pinholes, which is detrimental to device performance.

4.2. Optoelectronic and electronic devices
Forming PNSs is a novel approach to manipulate the optical and electronic properties, compared with the conventional doping strategy. By exploiting the talent of the PNSs, photodetectors, light-emitting diodes (LEDs), memory devices have been realized by a lot of groups, which will be discussed under this subtitle.

4.2.1. Photodetector. 
Photodetectors can convert light signal into electrical signal, which are widely used for imaging, security, and sensing purposes. Since the periodic nanostructured semiconductors demonstrate improved optoelectronic properties compared to their bulk competitors, they are able to serve as excellent candidates for photodetectors.
Type-II superlattices are suitable for infrared detection due to the tunable band structure towards a small band gap, reduced Auger recombination,332 enhanced light absorption due to the overlap of the electron–hole wavefunction and excellent material quality.333 Razeghi and coworkers reported on the InAs/InAs1−xSbx type-II superlattice nBn photodetector for its long wavelength infrared detection, comprising two n-doped superlattice active regions and a conduction-band-barrier.334 A thin layer of AlAs was introduced into the middle of the InAs layers, which separated the InAs quantum well and induced the conduction-band-barrier due to the large bandgap of AlAs. The barrier had a continuous valence band alignment with the n-doped active regions so that the diffusion of the holes was allowed, while the transportation of electrons was blocked. The device based on the InAs/InAs0.45Sb0.55 type-II superlattices exhibited a 50% cut-off wavelength of 10 μm at 77 K, which achieved the best responsivity (R) of 3.47 A W−1 under front side illumination without anti-reflection coating. The dark current density was characterized to be 4.4 × 10−4 A cm−2 at −90 mV bias at 77 K. The detectivity (D*) of the device stayed almost constant in the wavelength range from 5 μm to 9.5 μm at low biases between −100 mV and −50 mV, suggesting the potential in infrared imaging applications. InAs/InAsSb type-II superlattices are also applied to mid-infrared photodetectors. Evangelia et al. reported an innovative nBn-structure photodetector in which superlattices were grown in GaSb/AlSb/Si buffer by heteroepitaxial growth.335 AlSb/GaSb dislocation filters were used to introduce additional strain to promote the growth of GaSb on Si. Fig. 47a is the schematic diagram of the nBn-structure device, demonstrating a mid-infrared response (Fig. 47b). In the spectral range from 3.7 to 4.9 μm, the device presented a R of 0.88 A W−1, an EQE of 25.6%, a D* of 1.5 × 1010 Jones and a 50% cut-off wavelength at 5.4 μm under −100 mV bias at 200 K (Fig. 47c).
 Fig. 47  (a) Schematic diagram of the InAs/InAsSb type-II superlattice nBn structure grown on top of the GaSb/AlSb/Si buffer, using AlSb/GaSb dislocation filters. (b) Normalized R spectra for the detector at different temperatures (CO2 absorption shown in grey, water vapor absorption in blue). (c) Total spectral R and D* as a function of temperature in the 3.7 to 4.9 μm spectral band. The operation bias voltage for all measurements was 100 mV. Reprinted with permission from ref. 335. Copyright 2019, American Chemical Society. 
Nanowires presenting high photocurrent gain are promising for photodetector applications. Dai et al. first reported the CdS/CdS:SnS2 superlattice nanowires for UV photodetectors.135 The superlattice structure, which was synthesized by a micro-environment-controlled co-evaporation technique, had high purity with lengths of up to hundreds of μm, as shown in Fig. 48a and b. Photodetector devices were fabricated on p-type Si semiconductors with a SiO2 layer (Fig. 48c), which exhibited an on/off ratio of 105, a R of 2.5 × 103 A W−1, an EQE of 8.6 × 105 and a response time of 10 ms at 5 V bias under 365 nm excitation with the light intensity of 3.75 mW cm−2. This performance was much higher than that of the pure-CdS nanostructure-based photodetectors, and attributed to the efficient charge transport (Fig. 48d for superlattice device, Fig. 48e for pure-CdS device). Fig. 48f illustrates the band alignment of the superlattice nanowire, where a built-in electric field at the CdS/SnS2 interface promoted the transport of electrons to the CdS side and the holes to the CdS/SnS2 side.
 Fig. 48  (a) SEM images of the CdS/CdS:SnS2 superlattice nanowires; inset: the magnification SEM of the typical sample. (b) The XRD images of the CdS/CdS:SnS2 superlattice nanowires, showing narrow peaks. (c) Schematic of a CdS/CdS:SnS2 superlattice nanowire based photodetector. The bias-dependent photocurrent as a function of the incident light intensity (at 365 nm) of the (d) CdS/CdS:SnS2 superlattice nanowire-based photodetector, and (e) pure CdS nanowire-based photodetector. (f) Schematic diagram of the energy band for the CdS superlattice nanowire under light irradiation. Reprinted with permission from ref. 135. Copyright 2016, The Royal Society of Chemistry. (g) Schematic diagram of the single GaN nanowires with 20 periods of the AlN/GaN:Ge heterostructure in the AlN shell, and HAADF STEM images of two coalesced nanowires with different magnifications. The bright and dark grey areas correspond to GaN and AlN, respectively. Reprinted with permission from ref. 32. Copyright 2016, American Chemical Society. (h) Schematic diagrams of the evolution of the band diagram under (i) negative bias, (ii) zero bias, and (iii) positive bias. The Fermi levels are indicated by a dash-dotted blue line in each diagram. (i) Photocurrent spectra of a single nanowire at different biases (±0.5, ±1, and ±2 V). Solid (dashed) lines correspond to a positive (negative) bias. Shadowed areas outline the difference in the response between the positive and negative bias. (j) EBIC heat maps at (i) −2 V bias and (ii) +2 V bias superimposed on an SEM image of the nanowire under study. The location of the GaN/AlN superlattice is outlined with green vertical lines. Reprinted with permission from ref. 336. Copyright 2017, American Chemical Society. 
Single GaN nanowires with 20 periods of the AlN/GaN:Ge heterostructure in the AlN shell were developed for visible-band photodetectors (Fig. 48g).32 Due to the coalescence of nanowires with a displacement of the GaN:Ge nanodisks, higher dark current and lower photosensitivity factors were observed as the effective superlattice length was reduced by a smaller number of the active superlattice periods (Fig. 48g). This work indicates that the coalescence of nanowires would reduce the homogeneity, influencing the device performance and leading to low reproducibility. Maria and coworkers reported the single-nanowire photodetector based on GaN with 30 periods of GaN/AlN superlattices with good homogeneity.336 A highly-n-doped GaN capping segment was introduced into the structure, leading to a bias-dependent light response. As illustrated in Fig. 48h, there were two built-in electric fields existing in the system that were induced by the polarization differences between the GaN stem and the superlattice, and between the superlattice and the GaN cap. Under positive biases, a photocurrent was favorably generated in the GaN/AlN superlattice (Fig. 48j-i), while under negative biases, the collection of carriers in the GaN stem was favored (Fig. 48j-ii). As a result, the device showed enhanced responses in the 280–330 nm range at positive biases, and in the 330–360 nm range at negative biases (Fig. 48i).
Ho et al. fabricated a InGaZnO superlattice nanowire-based UV photodetector,337 where the superlattice structure consisted of In, Ga/Zn–O layers and In–O layers. They tuned the material properties by varying the Ga concentration in the superlattices, which subsequently changed the oxygen states with the reduced oxygen vacancies. The best photodetector performance was obtained with In1.8Ga1.8Zn2.4O7 superlattices, delivering an on/off ratio of 1.9 × 105, a R of 1.95 × 105 A W−1, an EQE of 9.28 × 107% and a rise time of 0.93 s at 1 V bias under 261 nm excitation with the light intensity of 2.3 mW cm−2. Interestingly, a transparent photodetector device was developed, which showed a transparency of over 80% in the visible light range. Wei et al. demonstrated highly-sensitive superlattice structure InSe-based photodetectors.192 At a bias of 1 V, their device showed a high R of 1.7 × 104 A W−1, a D* of 1.4 × 1013 Jones, an EQE of 1.7 × 107% and a response time of 20 ms, which is much better than the performance of pristine InSe-based photodetector devices. The superior device performance was largely contributed from the lower contact resistance, which was 2.4 kΩ, 210 times lower than that of pristine InSe.
Periodic-nanostructure integrated perovskite materials also present great potential in photodetector applications. In Li's work, a 1D nanograting bonded porous 2D photonic crystal perovskite photodetector demonstrated a R of 12.67 A W−1 and a D* of 3.22 × 1013 Jones at 10 V bias, under white light illumination of 32 μW cm−2, which was 6–7 times higher than the typical perovskite film-based device with a R of 1.82 A W−1 and a D* of 5.11 × 1012 Jones.198 This enhancement in performance resulted from the improved light absorbance by introducing highly ordered nanogratings and 2D porous structure. However, due to the limitation of the measurement set up, the response time was as long as 21 ms for the rise time and 67 ms for the decay time.


4.2.2. Light-emitting diodes. 
A light-emitting diode (LED) is a semiconductor light source, which emits when a certain current flows through it. It obtains several advantages over the traditional technology in terms of lower power consumption, longer lifetime, faster switching and other properties. The PNSs show great potential in the LED applications for its band-modulating capability.

Fig. 49a illustrates the device structure of a deep ultraviolet (DUV) LED, of which the n-type confinement layer consisted of stepped and 2 period superlattice structure of n-type Al0.6Ga0.4N/Al0.57Ga0.43N.338 The superlattices showed the band-modulating effect on the device. As shown in Fig. 49b, the introduction of a superlattice lowered the effective barrier height of the carriers, which subsequently increased the carrier concentration with efficient carrier injection, guaranteeing the high radiative recombination rate. The electrostatic fields in the QWs were also decreased, enhancing the confinement and reducing carrier leakage in the active region. The DUV LED had an emission peak at 289 nm with the maximum IQE of 53.9% and a light output power of 39.52 mW, which were improved by two times of those of the simple device (26.8% and 19.46 mW, respectively). Efforts were also made to modify the intermediate p-region in the DUV LEDs. Ramit et al. specially designed superlattice quaternary nitride alloy materials for the p-region.339 The material compositions are illustrated in Fig. 49c. All devices had an emission peak at 272.96 nm. The DUV LED device of structure D with p-Al0.62In0.03Ga0.35N/p-Al0.5In0.03Ga0.47N superlattice material with 5 periods demonstrated the best device performance. Its output power was 28.3 times that of the conventional DUV LED with structure A at the current density of 200 A cm−2, and the maximum internal quantum efficiency (IQE) achieved was 93.92%, as shown in Fig. 49d and e. Especially, the DUV LED in the structure had an efficiency drop of 0.84%, lowered by 99.08% in comparison to the conventional structured device, contributed from the lower energy barrier for the holes. A rational design of the composition and the period structure of the periodic nanostructure material provide more chances of performance enhancement.
 Fig. 49  (a) Schematic diagram of the DUV LED with n-type Al0.6Ga0.4N/Al0.57Ga0.43N superlattices. (b) Electrostatic fields in the QWs of the deep ultraviolet LED at 120 mA. Reprinted with permission from ref. 338. Copyright 2020, Elsevier. (c) Schematic diagrams of the four structures (A–D) of the DUV LEDs. (d) The output power and (e) IQE for the four LED structures as a function of the current density. Reprinted with permission from ref. 339. Copyright 2020, Elsevier. 
Paliwal et al. designed GaN/In0.18Al0.82N-based short period superlattices as a top waveguide core and cladding layers for LEDs emitting at 450 nm.340 As reviewed in sections 3.3 and 3.4, PNSs have a profound effect on the band structure. Here, the alternately arranged p-type GaN and In0.18Al0.82N layers induced the formation of a stepped mini-band structure, which increased the energy barrier height of the electrons towards the p-type contact side. At the same time, the band modulation also improved the hole transport, resulting in enhanced light output power (248 mW at 500 mA) and reduced dynamic resistance (0.94 Ω). It was also reported that an enhancement in the optical confinement factor from 3.28% to 3.42% led to better optical confinement.


4.2.3. Memory devices. 
Memory is one of the key components of electronic devices for information technology. Especially, random resistive access memory (RRAM) has been acknowledged as a promising candidate for the next-generation non-volatile memories. The RRAM is operated through resistive switching behaviors occurring between the high resistance state (HRS) and low resistance state (LRS), originating by the formation or deformation of a conductive filament across the dielectric materials or the generation of oxygen vacancies due to oxygen atom diffusion from the oxide-based dielectric materials.341–343 In designing RRAM devices, PNSs have attracted major interests due to its improved properties compared to their mixed counterparts, such as significant power efficiency and switching properties. The bipolar resistive-switching characteristics were observed in LA0.7Sr0.3MnO3/SrRuO3 superlattices.344 A planar-structure memory device with 30 layers of LA0.7Sr0.3MnO3/SrRuO3 presented a memory window, which was described by an on/off ratio of ∼14, and an endurance of 30–40 cycles. As an excellent semiconductor material, halide perovskites have also been employed in RRAM devices. Wang et al. first reported the memory application of the mixed-halide perovskite MAPbI3−xClx.345 The device with the structure of Au/MAPbI3−xClx/FTO showed a bipolar resistive switching (RS) characteristic at low bias (<1 V), keeping an endurance of over 100 cycles and a retention time of 104 s. However, the on/off ratio was relatively small, making it difficult to distinguish the storage information. A multi-level memory device was developed on layered 2D perovskite (BzA)2CuBr4 (BzA: benzylamine), which achieved an on/off ratio as high as 108.346Fig. 50a is the IV-curve of the voltage sweep of the device Ag/PMMA/(BzA)2CuBr4/Pt, of which the memory window is large. Furthermore, the device showed reliable HRS and LRS characteristics at different compliance currents, confirming its capacity for multi-level storage (Fig. 50b). The good stability of the 2D perovskites also equipped the device with high endurance of 2000 cycles and a retention time of over 1000 s.
 Fig. 50  (a) I–V curve of the Ag/PMMA/(BzA)2CuBr4/Pt memory device for the first voltage sweep, and (b) multi-level I–V curves measured at a compliance current from 10−5 A to 10−1 A with an interval of 10−1 A. Applied biases for SET and RESET ranged between +0.7 and −0.7 V. Reprinted with permission from ref. 346. Copyright 2020, John Wiley and Sons. (c) Schematic structure of the iPCM devices based on Ge–Te/Sb–Te superlattices. (d) Proposed switching model for iPCM. Ge, Sb, and Te atoms are shown in green, magenta and yellow color, respectively. (e) Time-domain profile of the applied voltage and the resulting current during the bipolar read–write process in iPCM. The zoomed areas at the bottom of the figure show the voltage and current profiles for the RESET and SET processes, and the zoomed area at the top shows the voltage and current profiles for the read process. The three dashed lines in the RESET and SET cases indicate the start of the applied switching voltage, the transition state during the switching process, and the end of that transition. The four dashed lines for the case of the read process indicate the start of the increase in the applied voltage, the maximum of the applied voltage, the start of the decrease in the applied voltage and the end of the read voltage pulse. Reprinted with permission from ref. 348. Copyright 2019, John Wiley and Sons. 
Interfacial phase change memory (iPCM) is another type of memory device, which is driven by the changes of the optoelectrical properties of the material with different phases. It was reported that the arrangement of Ge–Te–Sb into a superlattice structure can reduce the switching power consumption, as well as improve the electrical properties.347 Mitrofanov et al. demonstrated a high-speed iPCM device based on the Ge–Te/Sb–Te superlattices.348 The device structure is shown in Fig. 50c. In this case, there were two polarity-sensitive structural states in the Ge–Te/Sb–Te superlattices that could be tuned by the applied bias, leading to the bipolar switching. The transition between LRS and HRS originated from the switching of Ge–Te–Ge–Te/Te–Ge–Te–Ge stacking and Te–Ge–Ge–Te stacking (Fig. 50d).349 The superlattice device had an endurance of 104 cycles and a retention time of over 200 hours. Interestingly, the switching speed of the superlattice device was 1000 times faster than that of the conventional Ge–Sb–Te alloy-based iPCM (Fig. 50e).

5. Challenges and opportunities
In this review, we have discussed the advances in periodic nanostructured materials, focusing on their preparation methods, properties and applications in energy conversion, optoelectronics and electronics. PNSs provide a versatile platform for property modulation and various applications due to their influence on the properties at the atomic and molecular level, collective properties of the corresponding compositions, and synergistic effects. The examples discussed in this review have already showcased their great potential for advanced applications. However, there are still challenges that need to be overcome by providing great opportunities for further studies, and even towards commercialization as summarized below.
(1) The preparation strategies cover a wide range of methods, including vapour phase, solution phase and other complex methods. However, most of these methods suffered from flaws of low production efficiency, uncontrollable morphology and stability issues. For instance, the CVD method can precisely control the morphology, but it is hard to produce lateral PNSs in a large scale; the complex preparation methods used to synthesis DNA-mediated PNSs and most of the moiré superlattices result in low-scale production; the substrate-free self-assembly method cannot precisely control the repeating layers of 2D layered PNSs; and basically every supramolecular assembled materials will face the mechanical strength issue since the nature of non-covalent interactions cannot provide a permanent structure with high durability. Hence, in preparing PNSs, there are opportunities for future studies to either integrate the current preparation methods or develop new methods to balance these shortcomings. For instance, adding coatings to the self-assembled PNSs can permanently maintain their periodic structures. Introducing a microfluidics technique to prepare the supramolecular assembled PNSs can more precisely control the concentration and ratio of each component in a relatively small space, thereby controlling the composition of PNSs, as well as preserving the large-scale production.
(2) A variety of emergent properties have been investigated in various types of PNS, often properties that are absent in their parent compounds. However, the constituents of PNS are mostly restricted to one type of material, e.g., oxide superlattices, with the exception of hybrid organic-2D PNS. It will be interesting to further explore the formation of PNS by combining different classes of materials, e.g., 2D van der Waals materials and 2D metal oxides in terms of inducing new emergent properties by harnessing the synergistic effect. In the organic–inorganic hybrid PNS, the real atomic structure of the organic intercalation layer remains to be clarified. This is in stark contrast to, for example, oxide superlattices in which TEM provides an accurate description of the interfacial atomic arrangements. In addition, the intrinsic properties of the organic species are less explored in the hybrid PNS. It will be interesting to introduce the molecular species with emergent properties, such as magnetism, to combine with the 2D van der Waals layers to exploit the interfacial coupling for new functionalities. In terms of forming novel PNSs, besides the adjustment of compositions or dimensions, the idea of twisting the bilayer graphene with a certain angle opens up a new avenue to induce the emergent superconducting state or strongly correlated phases. This intriguing architecture greatly inspires researchers to try more 2D materials, such as TMDs. However, the fabrication of such structures remains laborious with very low device yields, placing significant challenges for the practical applications of twistronics. Innovations in the fabrication technology for the precise assembly of 2D van der Waals materials with arbitrary sequence and rotation are desired, for example, high throughput automation enabled through robotic arms and machine-learning approaches.
(3) The extraordinary properties of PNSs offer great potential for various applications, and current studies have already demonstrated their unique contribution. For instance, overall water splitting needs to integrate both HER and OER catalytic activities into one catalyst, which is the strength of PNSs since they can collect properties from each composition and enhance the performance via synergistic effects. Introducing PNSs with non-precious compositions to catalysts for fuel cells can reduce the ratio of precious metals in catalysts to further decrease their costs, and enhance their catalytic activity via synergistic effects. Importantly, the tunable optoelectrical properties from the easy-to-modulate periodic structure provide more opportunities to fabricate high-performance applications. By introducing PNSs, enhancements of the carrier transport and light absorption could be achieved for improving the performance of solar cells, LEDs, photodetectors and other optoelectronic devices. The modulation of bandgaps of PNSs can be well suited to the demand of photochemical catalysts, which is also favored in the fabrication of LEDs with desired emission peaks and photodetectors with specific detection range. However, an in-depth understanding of the effect of PNSs for various applications is still limited. For example, studies of the carrier dynamics in 2D perovskite materials are highly required. Although 2D perovskites exhibit superior stability over their 3D counterparts, the quantum confinement still hinders the PCE of solar cells. Performance breakthrough relies on the precise modulation of the optoelectrical properties for PNSs with solid knowledge of the structure.
In summary, faced with all of the benefits, challenges and opportunities of PNSs, the up-to-date studies have resulted in many breakthroughs. However, these breakthroughs are inadequate, and there still exists the gap between laboratory research and industrial applications. After comprehensive review and constructive presentation in this review, we hope to shed some light on the further advancement of next-generation PNSs to be built-up on novel advanced materials for the scientific communities of this specific research area. With further synergistic combination of the lateral development in data-driven materials discoveries, in situ microscopies and spectroscopies, and high throughput manufacturing, the development of PNS science and technology should be further accelerated in the near future.

Table Content:

Hang Yin
	Hang Yin is currently a PhD student at the Research School of Chemistry, Australian National University (Australia). He got his MSc from the University of Macau (China) in 2018 and BSc from Sichuan University (China) in 2015. His research interests lie in supramolecular chemistry, molecular machines, and energy conversion. He is now focusing on the development of novel nano materials for photocatalytic energy conversion.

Kaijian Xing
	Dr Kaijian Xing is a postdoc at ARC Centre of Excellent in Future Low-Energy Electronics Technologies at Monash University. He started Master study on nano-electronic engineering at the University of Melbourne. Afterwards he obtained the PhD degree in condensed matter physics at La Trobe University. He has been working on diamond electronics for the past 4 years, developing a solid-state surface transfer doping approach for controlling the diamond surface conductivity. The recent breakthrough made by him in the heterostructure between diamond and transition metal oxides paves the way for the high-performance diamond surface conducting devices as well as the spintronics.

Yurou Zhang
	Yurou Zhang received her BEng degree from East China University of Science and Technology in 2019. Since then, she has been pursuing her PhD degree under the supervision of Dr Jung Ho Yun at the University of Queensland. Her research interests mainly focus on optoelectronic properties and applications of organic–inorganic hybrid perovskite materials.

Jung-Ho Yun
	Jung-Ho Yun is currently an ARC DECRA Fellow in UQ Nanomaterials Centre, School of Chemical Engineering, the University of Queensland, Australia. He received his BSc and MSc degrees from Kyung Hee University, Republic of Korea, and PhD degree from the University of New South Wales, Australia in 2012. Prior to the PhD study, he also worked as a researcher to develop functional nanomaterials-based electrodes for electrochemical wastewater treatment systems at R&D Centre, NANOPAC Co., Ltd., Republic of Korea for 5 years. His research interests lie in designing nanostructured semiconducting materials toward solar energy conversion, environmental remediation, and electronic sensing applications.

Dong-Chen Qi
	Dongchen Qi is an Associate Professor and ARC Future Fellow at Queensland University of Technology. He received his BSc in Physics from Peking University (2003) and PhD in Physics from the National University of Singapore (2009). His research interests lie in the area of experimental condensed matter physics and surface science, focusing on creating, understanding, and controlling the surfaces and interfaces of functional materials at the nanoscale to develop new technologies and material platforms for the next-generation devices.

Zongyou Yin
	Associate Professor Zongyou Yin obtained his BS and MS degrees at Jilin University in China, and completed his PhD at Nanyang Technological University (NTU) in Singapore. Then, he started his postdoc careers at NTU/Singapore, IMRE/Singapore, followed by MIT and then Harvard University. Dr Yin started his own research group at Australian National Univesrity (ANU) from 2017. His group's research is interdisciplinary, encompassing the chemistry and physics of nano-to-atomic materials, fundamental relationship among materials-structures-devices, and synergistic integration of multi-functions towards systems for energy and wearable applications.

(e) Time-domain profile of the applied voltage and the resulting current during the bipolar read–write process in iPCM. The zoomed areas at the bottom of the figure show the voltage and current profiles for the RESET and SET processes, and the zoomed area at the top shows the voltage and current profiles for the read process. The three dashed lines in the RESET and SET cases indicate the start of the applied switching voltage, the transition state during the switching process, and the end of that transition. The four dashed lines for the case of the read process indicate the start of the increase in the applied voltage, the maximum of the applied voltage, the start of the decrease in the applied voltage and the end of the read voltage pulse.
